<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Middle Ages</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Period of European history from the 5th to the late 15th-century</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about medieval Europe. For a global history of the period between the 5th and 15th centuries, see <a href="/wiki/Post-classical_history" title="Post-classical history">Post-classical history</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Middle_Ages_(disambiguation)" title="Middle Ages (disambiguation)">Middle Ages (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Medieval times" redirects here. For the dinner theatre, see <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Times" title="Medieval Times">Medieval Times</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p><p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:JuengeresMathildenkreuz.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2403" data-file-width="1700" decoding="async" height="353" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/JuengeresMathildenkreuz.jpg/250px-JuengeresMathildenkreuz.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/JuengeresMathildenkreuz.jpg/375px-JuengeresMathildenkreuz.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/JuengeresMathildenkreuz.jpg/500px-JuengeresMathildenkreuz.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:JuengeresMathildenkreuz.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Cross_of_Mathilde" title="Cross of Mathilde">Cross of Mathilde</a>, a <i><a href="/wiki/Crux_gemmata" title="Crux gemmata">crux gemmata</a></i> made for <a href="/wiki/Mathilde,_Abbess_of_Essen" title="Mathilde, Abbess of Essen">Mathilde, Abbess of Essen</a> (973–1011), who is shown kneeling before the Virgin and Child in the <a href="/wiki/Vitreous_enamel" title="Vitreous enamel">enamel</a> plaque. The figure of <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Christ</a> is slightly later. Probably made in <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a> or <a href="/wiki/Essen" title="Essen">Essen</a>, the cross demonstrates several medieval techniques: <a href="/wiki/Casting" title="Casting">cast</a> figurative sculpture, <a href="/wiki/Filigree" title="Filigree">filigree</a>, enamelling, gem polishing and setting, and the reuse of Classical <a href="/wiki/Cameo_(carving)" title="Cameo (carving)">cameos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Engraved_gem" title="Engraved gem">engraved gems</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Europe" title="History of Europe">history of Europe</a>, the <b>Middle Ages</b> or <b>medieval period</b> lasted approximately from the 5th to the late 15th centuries, similar to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post-classical" title="Post-classical">post-classical period</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_history" title="Global history">global history</a>. It began with the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Western_Roman_Empire" title="Fall of the Western Roman Empire">fall of the Western Roman Empire</a> and transitioned into the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Discovery" title="Age of Discovery">Age of Discovery</a>. The Middle Ages is the middle period of the three traditional divisions of Western history: <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical antiquity</a>, the medieval period, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_history" title="Modern history">modern period</a>. The medieval period is itself subdivided into the <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">Early</a>, <a href="/wiki/High_Middle_Ages" title="High Middle Ages">High</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Late_Middle_Ages" title="Late Middle Ages">Late Middle Ages</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Population_decline" title="Population decline">Population decline</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Counterurbanisation" title="Counterurbanisation">counterurbanisation</a>, the collapse of centralized authority, invasions, and mass migrations of <a href="/wiki/Tribe" title="Tribe">tribes</a>, which had begun in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Antiquity" title="Late Antiquity">Late Antiquity</a>, continued into the Early Middle Ages. The large-scale movements of the <a href="/wiki/Migration_Period" title="Migration Period">Migration Period</a>, including various <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic peoples</a>, formed new kingdoms in what remained of the Western Roman Empire. In the 7th century, <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a> and the Middle East—most recently part of the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Eastern Roman (or Byzantine) Empire</a>—came under the rule of the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad Caliphate</a>, an Islamic empire, after conquest by <a href="/wiki/Rashidun" title="Rashidun">Muhammad's successors</a>. Although there were substantial changes in society and political structures, the break with <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical antiquity</a> was not complete. The still-sizeable Byzantine Empire, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Rome's</a> direct continuation, survived in the Eastern Mediterranean and remained a major power. Secular law was advanced greatly by the <i><a href="/wiki/Corpus_Juris_Civilis" title="Corpus Juris Civilis">Code of Justinian</a></i>. In the West, most kingdoms incorporated extant Roman institutions, while new bishoprics and monasteries were founded as Christianity expanded in Europe. The <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">Franks</a>, under the <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_dynasty" title="Carolingian dynasty">Carolingian dynasty</a>, briefly established the <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Empire" title="Carolingian Empire">Carolingian Empire</a> during the later 8th and early 9th centuries. It covered much of Western Europe but later succumbed to the pressures of internal civil wars combined with external invasions: <a href="/wiki/Vikings" title="Vikings">Vikings</a> from the north, <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_invasions_of_Europe" title="Hungarian invasions of Europe">Magyars</a> from the east, and <a href="/wiki/Saracen" title="Saracen">Saracens</a> from the south.
</p><p>During the High Middle Ages, which began after 1000, the population of Europe increased greatly as technological and <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_the_Middle_Ages#Technological_innovation" title="Agriculture in the Middle Ages">agricultural innovations</a> allowed trade to flourish and the <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Warm_Period" title="Medieval Warm Period">Medieval Warm Period</a> climate change allowed crop yields to increase. <a href="/wiki/Manorialism" title="Manorialism">Manorialism</a>, the organisation of <a href="/wiki/Peasant" title="Peasant">peasants</a> into villages that owed rent and labour services to the <a href="/wiki/Nobility" title="Nobility">nobles</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudalism</a>, the political structure whereby <a href="/wiki/Knight" title="Knight">knights</a> and lower-status nobles owed military service to their <a href="/wiki/Overlord" title="Overlord">overlords</a> in return for the right to rent from lands and <a href="/wiki/Manorialism" title="Manorialism">manors</a>, were two of the ways society was organised in the High Middle Ages. This period also saw the formal division of the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Orthodox</a> churches, with the <a href="/wiki/East%E2%80%93West_Schism" title="East–West Schism">East–West Schism of 1054</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Crusades</a>, which began in 1095, were military attempts by Western European Christians to regain control of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Land" title="Holy Land">Holy Land</a> from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslims</a>, and also contributed to the expansion of Latin Christendom in the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_region" title="Baltic region">Baltic region</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a>. Kings became the heads of centralised <a href="/wiki/Nation_state" title="Nation state">nation-states</a>, reducing crime and violence but making the ideal of a unified <a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Christendom</a> more distant. In the West, intellectual life was marked by <a href="/wiki/Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">scholasticism</a>, a philosophy that emphasised joining faith to reason, and by the founding of <a href="/wiki/University" title="University">universities</a>. The theology of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a>, the paintings of <a href="/wiki/Giotto" title="Giotto">Giotto</a>, the poetry of <a href="/wiki/Dante_Alighieri" title="Dante Alighieri">Dante</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Chaucer" title="Geoffrey Chaucer">Chaucer</a>, the travels of <a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">Marco Polo</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Gothic_architecture" title="Gothic architecture">Gothic architecture</a> of cathedrals such as <a href="/wiki/Chartres_Cathedral" title="Chartres Cathedral">Chartres</a> mark the end of this period.
</p><p>The Late Middle Ages was marked by difficulties and calamities including famine, plague, and war, which significantly diminished the population of Europe; between 1347 and 1350, the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a> killed about a third of Europeans. Controversy, <a href="/wiki/Heresy" title="Heresy">heresy</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Western_Schism" title="Western Schism">Western Schism</a> within the Catholic Church paralleled the interstate conflict, civil strife, and <a href="/wiki/Popular_revolts_in_late-medieval_Europe" title="Popular revolts in late-medieval Europe">peasant revolts</a> that occurred in the kingdoms. Cultural and technological developments transformed European society, concluding the Late Middle Ages and beginning the <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_period" title="Early modern period">early modern period</a>.
</p>

</ul>
</li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33"><a href="#Modern_perceptions"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Modern perceptions</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-34"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>

</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology_and_periodisation">Terminology and periodisation</span></h2>
<p>The Middle Ages is one of the three major periods in the most enduring scheme for analysing <a href="/wiki/History_of_Europe" title="History of Europe">European history</a>: <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical civilisation</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ancient_history" title="Ancient history">Antiquity</a>, the Middle Ages and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_history" title="Modern history">Modern Period</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Power304_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Power304-1">[1]</a></sup> The "Middle Ages" first appears in Latin in 1469 as <i>media tempestas</i> or "middle season".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miglio112_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Miglio112-2">[2]</a></sup> In early usage, there were many variants, including <i>medium aevum</i>, or "middle age", first recorded in 1604,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Albrow205_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Albrow205-3">[3]</a></sup> and <i>media saecula</i>, or "middle centuries", first recorded in 1625.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murray4_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Murray4-4">[4]</a></sup> The adjective "medieval" (or sometimes "mediaeval"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Random1194_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Random1194-5">[5]</a></sup> or "mediæval"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED290_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-OED290-6">[6]</a></sup> meaning pertaining to the Middle Ages, derives from <i>medium aevum</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Random1194_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Random1194-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Medieval writers divided history into periods such as the "<a href="/wiki/Six_Ages_of_the_World" title="Six Ages of the World">Six Ages</a>" or the "<a href="/wiki/Four_kingdoms_of_Daniel" title="Four kingdoms of Daniel">Four Empires</a>", and considered their time to be the last before the end of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mommsen236_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-mommsen236-7">[7]</a></sup> When referring to their own times, they spoke of them as being "modern".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dailyx_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dailyx-8">[8]</a></sup> In the 1330s,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (March 2022)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata"> – <a href="/wiki/Talk:Middle_Ages#Dubious" title="Talk:Middle Ages">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup> the Italian humanist and poet <a href="/wiki/Petrarch" title="Petrarch">Petrarch</a> referred to pre-Christian times as <i>antiqua</i> (or "ancient") and to the Christian period as <i>nova</i> (or "new").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-idea_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-idea-9">[9]</a></sup> Petrarch regarded the post-Roman centuries as "<a href="/wiki/Dark_Ages_(historiography)" title="Dark Ages (historiography)">dark</a>" compared to the "light" of <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical antiquity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mommsen227_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mommsen227-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (March 2022)">failed verification</span></a></i>]</sup> <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_Bruni" title="Leonardo Bruni">Leonardo Bruni</a> was the first historian to use <a href="/wiki/Late_Middle_Ages#Historiography_and_periodization" title="Late Middle Ages">tripartite periodisation</a> in his <i>History of the Florentine People</i> (1442), with a middle period "between the fall of the Roman Empire and the revival of city life sometime in late eleventh and twelfth centuries".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hankins_xvii_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hankins_xvii-11">[11]</a></sup> Tripartite <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Periodisation" title="Periodisation">periodisation</a> became standard after the 17th-century German historian <a href="/wiki/Christoph_Cellarius" title="Christoph Cellarius">Christoph Cellarius</a> divided history into three periods: ancient, medieval, and modern.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murray4_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Murray4-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>The most commonly given starting point for the Middle Ages is around 500,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> with the date of 476—the year the last Western Roman Emperor was deposed—first used by Bruni.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hankins_xvii_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hankins_xvii-11">[11]</a></sup> Later starting dates are sometimes used in the outer parts of Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> For Europe as a whole, 1500 is often considered to be the end of the Middle Ages,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> but there is no universally agreed upon end date. Depending on the context, events such as the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">conquest of Constantinople</a> by the Turks in 1453, <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Christopher Columbus</a>'s first voyage to the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a> in 1492,  or the <a href="/wiki/Reformation" title="Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a> in 1517 are sometimes used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies291_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davies291-15">[15]</a></sup> English historians often use the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bosworth_Field" title="Battle of Bosworth Field">Battle of Bosworth Field</a> in 1485 to mark the end of the period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> For Spain, dates commonly used are the death of King <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_II_of_Aragon" title="Ferdinand II of Aragon">Ferdinand II</a> in 1516, the death of Queen <a href="/wiki/Isabella_I_of_Castile" title="Isabella I of Castile">Isabella I of Castile</a> in 1504, or the <a href="/wiki/Granada_War" title="Granada War">conquest of Granada</a> in 1492.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Historians from <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance-speaking</a> countries tend to divide the Middle Ages into two parts: an earlier "High" and later "Low" period. English-speaking historians, following their German counterparts, generally subdivide the Middle Ages into three intervals: "Early", "High", and "Late".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Power304_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Power304-1">[1]</a></sup> In the 19th century, the entire Middle Ages were often referred to as the "<a href="/wiki/Dark_Ages_(historiography)" title="Dark Ages (historiography)">Dark Ages</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mommsen226_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-mommsen226-18">[18]</a></sup> but with the adoption of these subdivisions, use of this term was restricted to the Early Middle Ages, at least among historians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mommsen236_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-mommsen236-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (March 2022)">failed verification</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_Roman_Empire">Later Roman Empire</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Later_Roman_Empire" title="Later Roman Empire">Later Roman Empire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Venice_city_scenes_-_in_St._Mark%27s_square_-_St_Mark%27s_Basilica_(11002237996).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2121" data-file-width="1414" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Venice_city_scenes_-_in_St._Mark%27s_square_-_St_Mark%27s_Basilica_%2811002237996%29.jpg/220px-Venice_city_scenes_-_in_St._Mark%27s_square_-_St_Mark%27s_Basilica_%2811002237996%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Venice_city_scenes_-_in_St._Mark%27s_square_-_St_Mark%27s_Basilica_%2811002237996%29.jpg/330px-Venice_city_scenes_-_in_St._Mark%27s_square_-_St_Mark%27s_Basilica_%2811002237996%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Venice_city_scenes_-_in_St._Mark%27s_square_-_St_Mark%27s_Basilica_%2811002237996%29.jpg/440px-Venice_city_scenes_-_in_St._Mark%27s_square_-_St_Mark%27s_Basilica_%2811002237996%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Venice_city_scenes_-_in_St._Mark%27s_square_-_St_Mark%27s_Basilica_(11002237996).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A late Roman sculpture <a href="/wiki/Portrait_of_the_Four_Tetrarchs" title="Portrait of the Four Tetrarchs">depicting the Tetrarchs</a>, now in <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a>, Italy<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tansey242_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tansey242-19">[19]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> reached its greatest territorial extent during the 2nd century AD; the following two centuries witnessed the slow decline of Roman control over its outlying territories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cunliffe391_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cunliffe391-20">[20]</a></sup> Runaway inflation, external pressure on the frontiers, and <a href="/wiki/Plague_of_Cyprian" title="Plague of Cyprian">outbreaks of plague</a> combined to create the <a href="/wiki/Crisis_of_the_Third_Century" title="Crisis of the Third Century">Crisis of the Third Century</a>, with emperors coming to the throne only to be rapidly replaced by new usurpers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins3_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins3-21">[21]</a></sup> Military expenses increased steadily during the 3rd century, mainly in response to the <a href="/wiki/Roman%E2%80%93Persian_Wars#Roman–Sassanid_War" title="Roman–Persian Wars">war with</a> the newly established <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanian Empire</a> in the middle of the 3rd century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heather111_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heather111-22">[22]</a></sup> The army doubled in size, and cavalry and smaller units replaced the <a href="/wiki/Roman_legion" title="Roman legion">Roman legion</a> as the main tactical unit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown24-25_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown24-25-23">[23]</a></sup> The need for revenue led to increased taxes and a decline in numbers of the <a href="/wiki/Curiales" title="Curiales">curial</a>, or landowning, class, and decreasing numbers of them willing to shoulder the burdens of holding office in their native towns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heather111_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Heather111-22">[22]</a></sup> More bureaucrats were needed in the central administration to deal with the needs of the army, which led to complaints from civilians that there were more tax-collectors in the empire than tax-payers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown24-25_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brown24-25-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Emperor <a href="/wiki/Diocletian" title="Diocletian">Diocletian</a> (r. 284–305) split the empire into separately administered <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">eastern</a> and <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">western</a> halves in 286. This system, which eventually encompassed two <a href="/wiki/Augustus_(title)" title="Augustus (title)">senior co-emperors</a> and two <a href="/wiki/Caesar_(title)" title="Caesar (title)">junior co-emperors</a> (hence known as the <a href="/wiki/Tetrarchy" title="Tetrarchy">Tetrarchy</a>) stabilised the imperial government for about two decades. Diocletian's further reforms strengthened the governmental bureaucracy, reformed taxation, and strengthened the army, which bought the empire time but did not resolve the problems it was facing: excessive taxation, a declining birthrate, and pressures on its frontiers, among others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins8_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins8-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cunliffe403_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cunliffe403-25">[25]</a></sup> In 330, after a <a href="/wiki/Civil_wars_of_the_Tetrarchy" title="Civil wars of the Tetrarchy">period of civil war</a>, <a href="/wiki/Constantine_the_Great" title="Constantine the Great">Constantine the Great</a> (r. 306–337) refounded the city of <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">Byzantium</a> as the newly renamed eastern capital, <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins24_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins24-26">[26]</a></sup> For much of the 4th century, Roman society stabilised in a new form that differed from the earlier <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_ancient_Rome" title="Culture of ancient Rome">classical period</a>, with a widening gulf between the rich and poor, and a decline in the vitality of the smaller towns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown34_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown34-27">[27]</a></sup> Another change was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christianization_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="Christianization of the Roman Empire">Christianisation</a>, or conversion of the empire to <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>. The process was stimulated by the 3rd-century crisis, accelerated by the conversion of Constantine the Great, and by the end of the century Christianity emerged as the empire's dominant religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown65_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown65-28">[28]</a></sup> Debates about <a href="/wiki/Christian_theology" title="Christian theology">Christian theology</a>, customs and ethics intensified. Mainstream Christianity developed under imperial patronage, and those who persisted with theological views condemned at the Church leaders' general assemblies known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecumenical_councils" title="Ecumenical councils">ecumenical councils</a> had to endure official persecution. <a href="/wiki/Heresy_in_Christianity" title="Heresy in Christianity">Heretic views</a> could survive by popular support, or through intensive proselytizing activities. Examples include the uncompromisingly <a href="/wiki/Monophysitism" title="Monophysitism">Monophysite</a> Syrians and Egyptians, and the spread of <a href="/wiki/Arianism" title="Arianism">Arianism</a> among the Germanic peoples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Backman43_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Backman43-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins60_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins60-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>Civil war between rival emperors became common in the middle of the 4th century, diverting soldiers from the empire's frontier forces and allowing <a href="/wiki/Barbarian#Modern_academia" title="Barbarian">invaders</a> to encroach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins31_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins31-31">[31]</a></sup> Although the movements of peoples during this period are usually described as "invasions", they were not just military expeditions but <a href="/wiki/Migration_Period" title="Migration Period">migrations of entire peoples</a> into the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown122_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown122-32">[32]</a></sup> In 376, the <a href="/wiki/Goths" title="Goths">Goths</a>, fleeing from the <a href="/wiki/Huns" title="Huns">Huns</a>, received permission from Emperor <a href="/wiki/Valens" title="Valens">Valens</a> (r. 364–378) to settle in Roman territory in the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>. The settlement did not go smoothly, and when Roman officials mishandled the situation, the Goths began to raid and plunder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[A]</a></sup> Valens, attempting to put down the disorder, was killed fighting the Goths at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Adrianople" title="Battle of Adrianople">Battle of Adrianople</a> on 9 August 378.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heather145_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heather145-35">[34]</a></sup> In 401, the <a href="/wiki/Visigoths" title="Visigoths">Visigoths</a>, a Gothic group, invaded the Western Roman Empire and, although briefly forced back from Italy, in 410 <a href="/wiki/Sack_of_Rome_(410)" title="Sack of Rome (410)">sacked the city of Rome</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heather219_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heather219-36">[35]</a></sup> In 406 the <a href="/wiki/Alans" title="Alans">Alans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vandals" title="Vandals">Vandals</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Suebi" title="Suebi">Suevi</a> crossed into <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gaul</a>; over the next three years they spread across Gaul and in 409 crossed the <a href="/wiki/Pyrenees" title="Pyrenees">Pyrenees Mountains</a> into modern-day Spain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins59_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins59-37">[36]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">Franks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alemanni" title="Alemanni">Alemanni</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Burgundians" title="Burgundians">Burgundians</a> all ended up in Gaul while the <a href="/wiki/Angles" title="Angles">Angles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saxons" title="Saxons">Saxons</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jutes" title="Jutes">Jutes</a> <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_settlement_of_Britain" title="Anglo-Saxon settlement of Britain">settled in Britain</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cunliffe417_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cunliffe417-38">[37]</a></sup> and the Vandals went on to cross the strait of Gibraltar after which they conquered the province of <a href="/wiki/Africa_(Roman_province)" title="Africa (Roman province)">Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins80_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins80-39">[38]</a></sup> The Hunnic king <a href="/wiki/Attila" title="Attila">Attila</a> (r. 434–453) led invasions into the Balkans in 442 and 447, Gaul in 451, and Italy in 452. The Hunnic threat remained until Attila's death in 453, when the <a href="/wiki/Huns#Under_Attila" title="Huns">Hunnic confederation</a> he led fell apart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-James67_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-James67-40">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>When dealing with the migrations, the eastern and western elites applied different methods. The Eastern Romans combined the deployment of armed forces with gifts and grants of offices to the tribal leaders. The Western aristocrats failed to support the army but refused to pay tribute to prevent invasions by the tribes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown122_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brown122-32">[32]</a></sup> These invasions completely changed the political and demographic nature of the western section of the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cunliffe417_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cunliffe417-38">[37]</a></sup> By the end of the 5th century it was divided into smaller political units, ruled by the tribes that had invaded in the early part of the century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickham79_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wickham79-41">[40]</a></sup> The deposition of the last emperor of the west, <a href="/wiki/Romulus_Augustulus" title="Romulus Augustulus">Romulus Augustulus</a>, in 476 has traditionally marked the end of the Western Roman Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickham86_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wickham86-42">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[B]</a></sup> The Eastern Roman Empire, often referred to as the Byzantine Empire after the fall of its western counterpart, had little ability to assert control over the lost western territories. The <a href="/wiki/List_of_Byzantine_emperors" title="List of Byzantine emperors">Byzantine emperors</a> maintained a claim over the territory, but while none of the new kings in the west dared to elevate himself to the position of emperor of the west, Byzantine control of most of the Western Empire could not be sustained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins116_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins116-44">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Middle_Ages">Early Middle Ages</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">Early Middle Ages</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_realms">New realms</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Barbarian_kingdoms" title="Barbarian kingdoms">Barbarian kingdoms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Migration_Period" title="Migration Period">Migration Period</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Western_Roman_Empire" title="Fall of the Western Roman Empire">fall of the Western Roman Empire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Europe_and_the_Near_East_at_476_AD.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1967" data-file-width="2830" decoding="async" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Europe_and_the_Near_East_at_476_AD.png/260px-Europe_and_the_Near_East_at_476_AD.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Europe_and_the_Near_East_at_476_AD.png/390px-Europe_and_the_Near_East_at_476_AD.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Europe_and_the_Near_East_at_476_AD.png/520px-Europe_and_the_Near_East_at_476_AD.png 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Europe_and_the_Near_East_at_476_AD.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Barbarian_kingdoms" title="Barbarian kingdoms">Barbarian kingdoms</a> and tribes after the end of the Western Roman Empire</div></div></div>
<p>The emperors of the 5th century were often controlled by military strongmen such as <a href="/wiki/Stilicho" title="Stilicho">Stilicho</a> (d. 408), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flavius_Aetius" title="Flavius Aetius">Aetius</a> (d. 454), <a href="/wiki/Aspar" title="Aspar">Aspar</a> (d. 471), <a href="/wiki/Ricimer" title="Ricimer">Ricimer</a> (d. 472), or <a href="/wiki/Gundobad" title="Gundobad">Gundobad</a> (d. 516), who were partly or fully of non-Roman ancestry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickham96_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wickham96-45">[43]</a></sup> In the post-Roman world ethnic identities were flexible, often determined by loyalty to a successful military leader or by religion instead of ancestry or language. Ethnic markers quickly changed—by around 500, Arianism, originally a genuine Roman heresy, was associated with Germanic peoples, and the Goths rarely used <a href="/wiki/Gothic_language" title="Gothic language">their Germanic language</a> outside their churches. The fusion of Roman culture with the customs of the invading tribes is well documented. Popular assemblies that allowed free male tribal members more say in political matters than had been common in the Roman state developed into legislative and judicial bodies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickham98_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wickham98-46">[44]</a></sup> Material artefacts left by the Romans and the invaders are often similar, and tribal items were often modelled on Roman objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins100_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins100-47">[45]</a></sup> Much of the scholarly and written culture of the new kingdoms was also based on Roman intellectual traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins96_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins96-48">[46]</a></sup> An important difference was the gradual loss of tax revenue by the new polities. Many of the new political entities no longer supported their armies through taxes, instead relying on granting them land or rents. This meant there was less need for large tax revenues and so the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire#Taxation" title="Roman Empire">taxation systems</a> decayed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickham102_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wickham102-49">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Theoderic_Quarter_Siliqua_80000847.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="202" data-file-width="404" decoding="async" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Theoderic_Quarter_Siliqua_80000847.jpg/220px-Theoderic_Quarter_Siliqua_80000847.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Theoderic_Quarter_Siliqua_80000847.jpg/330px-Theoderic_Quarter_Siliqua_80000847.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/54/Theoderic_Quarter_Siliqua_80000847.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Theoderic_Quarter_Siliqua_80000847.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A coin of the <a href="/wiki/Ostrogoths" title="Ostrogoths">Ostrogothic</a> leader <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theoderic_the_Great" title="Theoderic the Great">Theoderic the Great</a>, struck in Milan, Italy, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> AD 491–501</span></div></div></div>
<p>Among the new peoples filling the political void left by Roman centralised government, the first Germanic groups now collectively known as <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxons</a> settled in Britain before the middle of the 5th century. The local culture had little impact on their way of life, but the linguistic assimilation of masses of the local <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Britons" title="Celtic Britons">Celtic Britons</a> to the newcomers is evident. By around 600, new political centres emerged, some local leaders accumulated considerable wealth, and <a href="/wiki/Heptarchy" title="Heptarchy">a number of small kingdoms</a> were formed. From among these realms, the kingdoms of <a href="/wiki/Northumbria" title="Northumbria">Northumbria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mercia" title="Mercia">Mercia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wessex" title="Wessex">Wessex</a>, and <a href="/wiki/East_Anglia" title="East Anglia">East Anglia</a> emerged as dominant powers by the end of the 7th century. Smaller kingdoms in present-day Wales and Scotland were still under the control of the native Britons and <a href="/wiki/Picts" title="Picts">Picts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickham156_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wickham156-50">[48]</a></sup> Ireland was divided into even smaller political units, usually known as tribal kingdoms, under the control of kings. There were perhaps as many as <a href="/wiki/List_of_Irish_kingdoms" title="List of Irish kingdoms">150 local kings</a> in Ireland, of varying importance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickham164_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wickham164-51">[49]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ostrogoths" title="Ostrogoths">Ostrogoths</a>, a Gothic tribe moved to Italy from the Balkans in the late 5th century under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theoderic_the_Great" title="Theoderic the Great">Theoderic the Great</a> (r. 493–526). He set up a <a href="/wiki/Ostrogothic_Kingdom" title="Ostrogothic Kingdom">kingdom</a> marked by its co-operation between the Italians and the Ostrogoths, at least until the last years of his reign. Power struggles between Romanized and traditionalist Ostrogothic groups followed his death, providing the opportunity for the Byzantines to <a href="/wiki/Gothic_War_(535%E2%80%93554)" title="Gothic War (535–554)">reconquer Italy</a> in the middle of 6th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-James82_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-James82-52">[50]</a></sup> The Burgundians settled in Gaul, and after an earlier realm was destroyed by the Huns in 436, formed a new kingdom in the 440s. Between today's <a href="/wiki/Geneva" title="Geneva">Geneva</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lyon" title="Lyon">Lyon</a>, it grew to become the realm of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_the_Burgundians" title="Kingdom of the Burgundians">Burgundy</a> in the late 5th and early 6th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-James77_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-James77-53">[51]</a></sup> Elsewhere in Gaul, the Franks and <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Britons" title="Celtic Britons">Celtic Britons</a> set up stable polities. <a href="/wiki/Francia" title="Francia">Francia</a> was centred in northern Gaul, and the first king of whom much is known is <a href="/wiki/Childeric_I" title="Childeric I">Childeric I</a> (d. 481).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[C]</a></sup> Under Childeric's son <a href="/wiki/Clovis_I" title="Clovis I">Clovis I</a> (r. 509–511), the founder of the <a href="/wiki/Merovingian_dynasty" title="Merovingian dynasty">Merovingian dynasty</a>, the Frankish kingdom expanded and converted to Christianity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-James79_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-James79-56">[53]</a></sup> Unlike other Germanic peoples, the Franks accepted Catholicism which facilitated their cooperation with the native <a href="/wiki/Gallo-Roman_culture" title="Gallo-Roman culture">Gallo-Roman</a> aristocracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[54]</a></sup> Britons fleeing from <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Britannia</i></span> – modern-day Great Britain – settled in what is now <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[D]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-James78_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-James78-58">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other monarchies were established by the Visigoths in the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Suebi" title="Suebi">Suebi</a> in northwestern Iberia, and the Vandals in <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-James77_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-James77-53">[51]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Lombards" title="Lombards">Lombards</a> settled in <a href="/wiki/Diocese_of_Pannonia" title="Diocese of Pannonia">Pannonia</a>, but the influx of the nomadic <a href="/wiki/Pannonian_Avars" title="Pannonian Avars">Avars</a> from the Asian steppes to Central Europe forced them to move on to <a href="/wiki/Northern_Italy" title="Northern Italy">Northern Italy</a> in 568. Here they conquered the lands once held by the Ostrogoths from the Byzantines, and established a <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_the_Lombards" title="Kingdom of the Lombards">new kingdom</a> composed of <a href="/wiki/Duke_(Lombard)" title="Duke (Lombard)">town-based duchies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins196_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins196-60">[56]</a></sup> By the end of the 6th century, the Avars conquered most <a href="/wiki/Early_Slavs" title="Early Slavs">Slavic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">Turkic</a> and Germanic tribes in the lowlands along the Lower and Middle Danube, and they were routinely able to force the Eastern emperors to pay tribute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Curta51_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Curta51-61">[57]</a></sup> Around 670, another steppe people, the <a href="/wiki/Bulgars" title="Bulgars">Bulgars</a> settled at the <a href="/wiki/Danube_Delta" title="Danube Delta">Danube Delta</a>. In 681, they <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ongal" title="Battle of Ongal">defeated</a> a Byzantine imperial army, and established a <a href="/wiki/First_Bulgarian_Empire" title="First Bulgarian Empire">new empire</a> on the Lower Danube, subjugating the local Slavic tribes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Curta71_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Curta71-62">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the invasions, some regions received a larger influx of new peoples than others. In Gaul for instance, the invaders settled much more extensively in the north-east than in the south-west. Slavs settled in <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a> and the Balkan Peninsula. The settlement of peoples was accompanied by changes in languages. <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, the literary language of the Western Roman Empire, was gradually replaced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vernacular_languages" title="Vernacular languages">vernacular languages</a> which evolved from Latin, but were distinct from it, collectively known as <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance languages</a>. These changes from Latin to the new languages took many centuries. Greek remained the language of the Byzantine Empire, but the migrations of the Slavs expanded the area of <a href="/wiki/Slavic_languages" title="Slavic languages">Slavic languages</a> in Eastern Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies235_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davies235-63">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Byzantine_survival">Byzantine survival</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire_under_the_Justinian_dynasty" title="Byzantine Empire under the Justinian dynasty">Byzantine Empire under the Justinian dynasty</a> and <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire_under_the_Heraclian_dynasty" title="Byzantine Empire under the Heraclian dynasty">Byzantine Empire under the Heraclian dynasty</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sanvitale03.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5752" data-file-width="9228" decoding="async" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Sanvitale03.jpg/220px-Sanvitale03.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Sanvitale03.jpg/330px-Sanvitale03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Sanvitale03.jpg/440px-Sanvitale03.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sanvitale03.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Mosaic" title="Mosaic">mosaic</a> showing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Justinian" title="Justinian">Justinian</a> with <a href="/wiki/Maximianus_of_Ravenna" title="Maximianus of Ravenna">the bishop</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ravenna" title="Ravenna">Ravenna</a> (Italy), bodyguards, and courtiers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adams158_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Adams158-64">[60]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>As Western Europe witnessed the formation of new kingdoms, the Eastern Roman Empire remained intact and experienced an economic revival that lasted into the early 7th century. There were fewer invasions of the eastern section of the empire; most occurred in the Balkans. Peace with the Sasanian Empire, the traditional enemy of Rome, lasted throughout most of the 5th century. The Eastern Empire was marked by closer relations between the political state and Christian Church, with doctrinal matters assuming an importance in Eastern politics that they did not have in Western Europe. Legal developments included the codification of <a href="/wiki/Roman_law" title="Roman law">Roman law</a>; the first effort—the <i><a href="/wiki/Codex_Theodosianus" title="Codex Theodosianus">Codex Theodosianus</a></i>—was completed in 438.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickham81_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wickham81-65">[61]</a></sup> Under Emperor <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian</a> (r. 527–565), a more comprehensive compilation took place—the <i><a href="/wiki/Corpus_Juris_Civilis" title="Corpus Juris Civilis">Corpus Juris Civilis</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Backman130_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Backman130-66">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>Justinian almost lost his throne during the <a href="/wiki/Nika_riots" title="Nika riots">Nika riots</a>, a popular revolt of elementary force that destroyed half of Constantinople in 532. After crushing the revolt, he reinforced the autocratic elements of the imperial government and mobilized his troops against the heretic western realms. The general <a href="/wiki/Belisarius" title="Belisarius">Belisarius</a> (d. 565) conquered <a href="/wiki/Vandalic_War" title="Vandalic War">North Africa from the Vandals</a>, and attacked the Ostrogoths, but the Italian campaign was interrupted due to an unexpected Sasanian invasion from the east. For the movement of troops from the Balkan provinces left the region virtually unprotected, the neighboring Slavic and Turkic tribes intensified their plundering raids across the Danube. Between 541 and 543, a deadly <a href="/wiki/Plague_of_Justinian" title="Plague of Justinian">outbreak of plague</a> decimated the empire's population, and the epidemic swept through the Mediterranean several times during the following decades. Justinian was to apply new methods to counterbalance its negative effects. He covered the lack of military personnel by developing an extensive system of border forts. To reduce fiscal deficit, he nationalized the silk industry and ceased to finance the maintenance of public roads. In a decade, he resumed expansionism, completing the conquest of the Ostrogothic kingdom, and seizing much of southern Spain from the Visigoths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown150_156_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown150_156-67">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>Justinian's reconquests and excessive building program have been criticised by historians for bringing his realm to the brink of bankruptcy, but many of the difficulties faced by Justinian's successors were probably due to other factors, including the epidemic. An additional problem to face the empire came as a result of the massive expansion of the Avars and their Slav allies. They conquered the Balkans and Greece with the exception of a few coastal cities before their <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Constantinople_(626)" title="Siege of Constantinople (626)">assault on Constantinople</a> was repulsed in 626.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown8_10_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown8_10-68">[64]</a></sup> In the east, border defences collapsed during a <a href="/wiki/Byzantine%E2%80%93Sasanian_War_of_602%E2%80%93628" title="Byzantine–Sasanian War of 602–628">new war with the Sasanian Empire</a> and the Persians seized last chunks of the empire, including Egypt, Syria, and much of <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>. A Persian army approached Constantinople to join the Avars and Slavs during the siege but a Byzantine fleet prevented them from crossing the <a href="/wiki/Bosporus" title="Bosporus">Bosporus</a> in 626. Two years later, Emperor <a href="/wiki/Heraclius" title="Heraclius">Heraclius</a> (r. 610–641) launched an unexpected counterattack against the heart of the Sassanian Empire bypassing the Persian army in the mountainous regions of Anatolia. He triumphed and the empire recovered all of its lost territories in the east in a new peace treaty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins140_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins140-69">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Western_society">Western society</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Early_medieval_European_dress" title="Early medieval European dress">Early medieval European dress</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medieval_cuisine" title="Medieval cuisine">medieval cuisine</a></div>
<p>In Western Europe, some of the older Roman elite families died out while others became more involved with ecclesiastical than secular affairs. Values attached to <a href="/wiki/Latin_literature" title="Latin literature">Latin scholarship</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Education_in_Ancient_Rome" title="Education in Ancient Rome">education</a> mostly disappeared, and while literacy remained important, it became a practical skill rather than a sign of elite status. In the 4th century, <a href="/wiki/Jerome" title="Jerome">Jerome</a> (d. 420) dreamed that God rebuked him for spending more time reading <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a> than the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a>. By the 6th century, <a href="/wiki/Gregory_of_Tours" title="Gregory of Tours">Gregory of Tours</a> (d. 594) had a similar dream, but instead of being chastised for reading Cicero, he was chastised for learning <a href="/wiki/Shorthand" title="Shorthand">shorthand</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown174_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown174-70">[66]</a></sup> By the late 6th century, the principal means of religious instruction in the Church had become music and art rather than the book.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown181_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown181-71">[67]</a></sup> Most intellectual efforts went towards imitating classical scholarship, but some <a href="/wiki/Early_medieval_literature" title="Early medieval literature">original works</a> were created, along with now-lost oral compositions. The writings of <a href="/wiki/Sidonius_Apollinaris" title="Sidonius Apollinaris">Sidonius Apollinaris</a> (d. 489), <a href="/wiki/Cassiodorus" title="Cassiodorus">Cassiodorus</a> (d. <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 585</span>), and <a href="/wiki/Boethius" title="Boethius">Boethius</a> (d. <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 525</span>) were typical of the age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown45_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown45-72">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>Changes also took place among laymen, as aristocratic culture focused on great feasts held in halls rather than on literary pursuits. Clothing for the elites was richly embellished with jewels and gold. Lords and kings supported entourages of fighters who formed the backbone of the military forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[E]</a></sup> Family ties within the elites were important, as were the virtues of loyalty, courage, and honour. These ties led to the prevalence of the feud in aristocratic society, examples of which included those related by Gregory of Tours that took place in <a href="/wiki/Merovingian_dynasty" title="Merovingian dynasty">Merovingian</a> Gaul. Most feuds seem to have ended quickly with the payment of some sort of <a href="/wiki/Weregild" title="Weregild">compensation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickham189_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wickham189-76">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>Women took part in aristocratic society mainly in their roles as wives and mothers of men, with the role of mother of a ruler being especially prominent in Merovingian Gaul. In Anglo-Saxon society the lack of many child rulers meant a lesser role for women as queen mothers, but this was compensated for by the increased role played by <a href="/wiki/Abbess" title="Abbess">abbesses</a> of monasteries. In contrast, in medieval Italy women were always considered under the protection and control of a male relative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickham195_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wickham195-77">[72]</a></sup> Women's influence on politics was particularly fragile, and early medieval authors tended to depict powerful women in a bad light. Examples include the Arian queen <a href="/wiki/Goiswintha" title="Goiswintha">Goiswintha</a> (d. 589), a vehement but unsuccessful opponent of the Visigoth's conversion to Catholicism, and the Frankish queen <a href="/wiki/Brunhilda_of_Austrasia" title="Brunhilda of Austrasia">Brunhilda of Austrasia</a> (d. 613) who was torn to pieces by horses after her enemies captured her at the age of 70.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickham116_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wickham116-78">[73]</a></sup> Women usually died at considerably younger age than men, primarily due to <a href="/wiki/Infanticide" title="Infanticide">infanticide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maternal_death" title="Maternal death">complacations at childbirth</a>. Infanticide was not an unusual practice in times of famine, and daughters fell victim to it more frequently than their brothers who could potentially do harder works. The disparity between the numbers of marriageable women and grown men led to the detailed regulation of legal institutions protecting women's interests, including the <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Morgengabe" title="Morgengabe">Morgengabe</a></i></span>, or "morning gift", a compensation for the loss of virginity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Backman_120_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Backman_120-79">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fr%C3%BChmittelalterliches_Dorf.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1668" data-file-width="2751" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Fr%C3%BChmittelalterliches_Dorf.jpg/220px-Fr%C3%BChmittelalterliches_Dorf.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Fr%C3%BChmittelalterliches_Dorf.jpg/330px-Fr%C3%BChmittelalterliches_Dorf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Fr%C3%BChmittelalterliches_Dorf.jpg/440px-Fr%C3%BChmittelalterliches_Dorf.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fr%C3%BChmittelalterliches_Dorf.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reconstruction of an early medieval peasant village in Bavaria</div></div></div>
<p>Peasant society is much less documented than the nobility. Most of the surviving information available to historians comes from <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeology</a>; few detailed written records documenting peasant life remain from before the 9th century. Most of the descriptions of the lower classes come from either <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Code_(law)" title="Code (law)">law codes</a> or writers from the upper classes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickham204_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wickham204-80">[75]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Landholding" title="Landholding">Landholding</a> patterns were not uniform; some areas had greatly fragmented landholding patterns, but in other areas large contiguous blocks of land were the norm. These differences allowed for a wide variety of peasant societies, some dominated by aristocratic landholders and others having a great deal of autonomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickham205_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wickham205-81">[76]</a></sup> Land settlement also varied greatly. Some peasants lived in large settlements that numbered as many as 700 inhabitants. Others lived in small groups of a few families and still others lived on isolated farms spread over the countryside. There were also areas where the pattern was a mix of two or more of those systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickham211_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wickham211-82">[77]</a></sup> Legislation made a clear distinction between free and unfree, but there was no sharp break between the legal status of the free peasant and the aristocrat, and it was possible for a free peasant's family to rise into the aristocracy over several generations through military service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickham215_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wickham215-83">[78]</a></sup> Demand for slaves was covered through warring and raids. Initially, the Franks' expansion and conflicts between the Anglo-Saxon realms supplied the slave market with prisoners of war and captives. After the Anglo-Saxons' conversion to Christianity, slave hunters mainly targeted the pagan Slav tribes—hence the English word "slave" from <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">slavicus</i></span>, the <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Latin" title="Medieval Latin">Medieval Latin</a> term for Slavs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dict261_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dict261-85">[80]</a></sup> Christian ethics brought about significant changes in the position of slaves in the 7th and 8th centuries. They were no more regarded as their lords' property, and their right to a decent treatment was enacted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Backman119_120_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Backman119_120-86">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>Roman city life and culture changed greatly in the early Middle Ages. Although Italian cities remained inhabited, they contracted significantly in size. Rome, for instance, shrank from a population of hundreds of thousands to around 30,000 by the end of the 6th century. <a href="/wiki/Roman_temple" title="Roman temple">Roman temples</a> were converted into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christianised_sites" title="Christianised sites">Christian churches</a> and city walls remained in use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown24-26_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown24-26-87">[82]</a></sup> In Northern Europe, cities also shrank, while civic monuments and other public buildings were raided for building materials. The establishment of new kingdoms often meant some growth for the towns chosen as capitals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-City3_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-City3-88">[83]</a></sup> Although there had been <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jews_in_the_Roman_Empire" title="Jews in the Roman Empire">Jewish communities in many Roman cities</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a> suffered periods of persecution after the conversion of the empire to Christianity. Officially they were tolerated, if subject to conversion efforts, and at times were even encouraged to settle in new areas<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The article does not mention the social and economic role of Jews in early medieval society. (August 2022)">why?</span></a></i>]</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jews191_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jews191-89">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rise_of_Islam">Rise of Islam</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Spread_of_Islam" title="Spread of Islam">Spread of Islam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Early_Muslim_conquests" title="Early Muslim conquests">Early Muslim conquests</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="413" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg/290px-Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg/435px-Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg/580px-Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg.png 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Early_Muslim_conquests" title="Early Muslim conquests">early Muslim conquests</a> <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r981673959">.mw-parser-output .legend{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .legend-color{display:inline-block;min-width:1.25em;height:1.25em;line-height:1.25;margin:1px 0;text-align:center;border:1px solid black;background-color:transparent;color:black}.mw-parser-output .legend-text{}</style><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#a1584e; color:white;"> </span> Expansion under Muhammad, 622–632</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ef9070; color:black;"> </span> Expansion during the Rashidun Caliphate, 632–661</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#fad07d; color:black;"> </span> Expansion during the Umayyad Caliphate, 661–750</div></div></div></div>
<p>Religious beliefs were in flux in the lands along the Eastern Roman and Persian frontiers during the late 6th and early 7th centuries. State-sponsored Christian missionaries proselytised among the pagan steppe peoples, and the Persians made attempts to enforce their <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a> on the Christian <a href="/wiki/Armenians" title="Armenians">Armenians</a>. <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a> was an active proselytising faith, and at least one <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arab</a> political leader—<a href="/wiki/Dhu_Nuwas" title="Dhu Nuwas">Dhu Nuwas</a>, ruler of what is today <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a>—converted to it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins136_141_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins136_141-90">[85]</a></sup> The emergence of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> in Arabia during the lifetime of <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> (d. 632) brought about more radical changes. After his death, Islamic forces conquered much of the Near East, starting with Syria <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_the_Levant" title="Muslim conquest of the Levant">in 634–35</a>, continuing with Persia <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Persia" title="Muslim conquest of Persia">between 637 and 642</a>, and reaching Egypt <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Egypt" title="Muslim conquest of Egypt">in 640–41</a>. In the eastern Mediterranean, the Muslim expansion was halted at Constantinople. The Eastern Romans used the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_Fire" title="Greek Fire">Greek Fire</a>, a highly combustible liquid, to defend their capital <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Constantinople_(674%E2%80%93678)" title="Siege of Constantinople (674–678)">in 674–78</a> and <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Constantinople_(717%E2%80%93718)" title="Siege of Constantinople (717–718)">717–18</a>. In the west, the advance of Islamic troops continued. They conquered North Africa <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_the_Maghreb" title="Muslim conquest of the Maghreb">by the early 8th century</a>, annihilated the Visigothic Kingdom <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_conquest_of_Hispania" title="Umayyad conquest of Hispania">in 711</a>, and invaded southern France <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_invasion_of_Gaul" title="Umayyad invasion of Gaul">in 713–25</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins142_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins142-91">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cunliffe421_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cunliffe421-92">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Muslim conquerors bypassed the mountainous northwestern region of the Iberian Peninsula. Here a small kingdom, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Asturias" title="Kingdom of Asturias">Asturias</a> emerged as the centre of local resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins376_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins376-93">[88]</a></sup> The defeat of Muslim forces at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tours" title="Battle of Tours">Battle of Tours</a> in 732 led to the reconquest of southern France by the Franks, but the main reason for the halt of Islamic growth in Europe was the overthrow of the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad Caliphate</a> and its replacement by the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid Caliphate</a>. The Abbasids moved their capital to <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a> and were more concerned with the Middle East than Europe, losing control of sections of the Muslim lands. Umayyad descendants took over <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Al-Andalus</a> (or Muslim Spain), the <a href="/wiki/Aghlabids" title="Aghlabids">Aghlabids</a> controlled North Africa, and the <a href="/wiki/Tulunids" title="Tulunids">Tulunids</a> became rulers of Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown15_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown15-94">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trade_and_economy">Trade and economy</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval_economic_history" title="Medieval economic history">Medieval economic history</a></div>
<p>The migrations and invasions of the 4th and 5th centuries disrupted trade networks around the Mediterranean. African goods stopped being imported into Europe, first disappearing from the interior and by the 7th century found only in a few cities such as Rome or <a href="/wiki/Naples" title="Naples">Naples</a>. By the end of the 7th century, under the impact of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_conquests" title="Muslim conquests">Muslim conquests</a>, African products were no longer found in Western Europe. The replacement of goods from long-range trade with local products was a trend throughout the old Roman lands that happened in the Early Middle Ages. This was especially marked in the lands that did not lie on the Mediterranean, such as northern Gaul or Britain. Non-local goods appearing in the archaeological record are usually luxury goods or metalworks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickham218_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wickham218-95">[90]</a></sup> In the 7th and 8th centuries, new commercial networks were developing in northern Europe. Goods like furs, <a href="/wiki/Walrus_ivory" title="Walrus ivory">walrus ivory</a> and amber were delivered from the Baltic region to western Europe, contributing to the development of new trade centers in East Anglia, northern Francia and Scandinavia. Conflicts over the control of trade routes and toll stations were common, and those who failed turned to raiding or settled in foreign lands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins347_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins347-96">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>The flourishing Islamic economies' constant demand for fresh labour force and raw materials opened up a new market for Europe around 750. Europe emerged as a major supplier of <a href="/wiki/House_slave" title="House slave">house slaves</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mamluk" title="Mamluk">slave soldiers</a> for Al-Andalus, northern Africa and the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>. Located in the vicinity of the Central European slave hunting areas, <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a> developed into the most important European center of slave trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[93]</a></sup> In addition, timber, fur and arms were delivered from Europe to the Mediterranean, while Europe imported spices, medicine, incense, and silk from the Levant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[94]</a></sup> The demand for exotic merchandise was reinforced primarily by internal factors, like population growth, and improved agricultural productivity. The large rivers connecting distant regions facilitated the expansion of transcontinental trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[95]</a></sup> Contemporaneous reports indicate that Anglo-Saxon merchants visited fairs at Paris, pirates preyed on tradesman travelling on the Danube, and Eastern Frankish merchants reached as far as <a href="/wiki/Zaragoza" title="Zaragoza">Zaragoza</a> in Al-Andalus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>The various Germanic states in the west all had <a href="/wiki/Coin" title="Coin">coinages</a> that imitated existing Roman and Byzantine forms. Gold continued to be minted until the end of the 7th century in 693-94 when it was replaced by silver in the Merovingian kingdom. The basic Frankish silver coin was the <a href="/wiki/Denarius" title="Denarius">denarius</a> or <a href="/wiki/French_denier" title="French denier">denier</a>, while the Anglo-Saxon version was called a <a href="/wiki/Penny" title="Penny">penny</a>. From these areas, the denier or penny spread throughout Europe from 700 to 1000 AD. Copper or bronze coins were not struck, nor were gold except in Southern Europe. No silver coins denominated in multiple units were minted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coin_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coin-102">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Church_and_monasticism">Church and monasticism</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Christianity in the Middle Ages">Christianity in the Middle Ages</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gregory_I_-_Antiphonary_of_Hartker_of_Sankt_Gallen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6376" data-file-width="5272" decoding="async" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Gregory_I_-_Antiphonary_of_Hartker_of_Sankt_Gallen.jpg/170px-Gregory_I_-_Antiphonary_of_Hartker_of_Sankt_Gallen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Gregory_I_-_Antiphonary_of_Hartker_of_Sankt_Gallen.jpg/255px-Gregory_I_-_Antiphonary_of_Hartker_of_Sankt_Gallen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Gregory_I_-_Antiphonary_of_Hartker_of_Sankt_Gallen.jpg/340px-Gregory_I_-_Antiphonary_of_Hartker_of_Sankt_Gallen.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gregory_I_-_Antiphonary_of_Hartker_of_Sankt_Gallen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An 11th-century illustration of <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_I" title="Pope Gregory I">Gregory the Great</a> dictating to a secretary</div></div></div>
<p>The idea of Christian unity endured although differences in ideology and practice between the Eastern and Western Churches became apparent by the 6th century. The formation of new realms reinforced the traditional Christian concept of the <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_church_and_state" title="Separation of church and state">separation of church and state</a> in the west, whereas this notion was alien to eastern clergymen who regarded the Roman state as an instrument of divine providence. In the Eastern Christians' view, an individual could be saved from sin through direct <a href="/wiki/Theosis_(Eastern_Christian_theology)" title="Theosis (Eastern Christian theology)">mystical communication</a> with God, but western clerics tended to regard themselves as unavoidable intercessors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown41_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown41-103">[98]</a></sup> In the late 7th century, <a href="/wiki/Clerical_marriage" title="Clerical marriage">clerical marriage</a> emerged as a permanent focus of controversy: the <a href="/wiki/Latin_Church" title="Latin Church">Latin Church</a> promoted <a href="/wiki/Clerical_celibacy_in_the_Catholic_Church" title="Clerical celibacy in the Catholic Church">complete celibacy</a> while the eastern clergy insisted on the more tolerant traditional approach. After the Muslim conquest of the Levant, the Byzantine emperors could less effectively intervene in the west. When <a href="/wiki/Leo_III_the_Isaurian" title="Leo III the Isaurian">Leo III</a> (r. 717–741) prohibited the display of paintings representing human figures in places of worship, the <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">papacy</a> openly censured the emperor's <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Iconoclasm" title="Byzantine Iconoclasm">iconoclast doctrine</a> and  <a href="/wiki/Caesaropapism" title="Caesaropapism">his claim</a> to declare new dogmas by imperial edicts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins218_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins218-104">[99]</a></sup> Although the Byzantine Church condemned iconoclasm in 843, further issues such as fierce rivalry for ecclesiastic jurisdiction over newly converted peoples, and the <a href="/wiki/Filioque" title="Filioque">unilateral modification</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Nicene_Creed" title="Nicene Creed">Nicene Creed</a> in the west widened to the extent that the differences were greater than the similarities. The decisive break, known as the <a href="/wiki/East%E2%80%93West_Schism" title="East–West Schism">East–West Schism</a>, came in 1054, when the papacy and the <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Patriarch_of_Constantinople" title="Ecumenical Patriarch of Constantinople">patriarchy of Constantinople</a> clashed over <a href="/wiki/Papal_supremacy" title="Papal supremacy">papal supremacy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Excommunication" title="Excommunication">excommunicated</a> each other, which led to the division of Christianity into two Churches—the Western branch became the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a> and the Eastern branch the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Church</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown45_46_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown45_46-105">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>The ecclesiastical structure of the Roman Empire survived the movements and invasions in the west mostly intact, but the papacy was little regarded, and few of the Western <a href="/wiki/Bishop" title="Bishop">bishops</a> looked to the bishop of Rome for religious or political leadership. <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Papacy" title="Byzantine Papacy">Many of the popes</a> prior to 750 were more concerned with Byzantine affairs and Eastern theological controversies. The register, or archived copies of the letters, of Pope <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_I" title="Pope Gregory I">Gregory the Great</a> (pope 590–604) survived, and of those more than 850 letters, the vast majority were concerned with affairs in Italy or Constantinople. The only part of Western Europe where the papacy had influence was Britain, where Gregory had sent the <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_mission" title="Gregorian mission">Gregorian mission</a> in 597 to convert the Anglo-Saxons to Christianity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WIckham170_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-WIckham170-106">[101]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hiberno-Scottish_mission" title="Hiberno-Scottish mission">Irish missionaries</a> were most active in Western Europe between the 5th and the 7th centuries, going first to England and Scotland and then on to the continent.
