Under such <a href="/wiki/Monk" title="Monk">monks</a> as <a href="/wiki/Columba" title="Columba">Columba</a> (d. 597) and <a href="/wiki/Columbanus" title="Columbanus">Columbanus</a> (d. 615), they founded monasteries, taught in Latin and Greek, and authored secular and religious works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Colish62_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Colish62-107">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Early Middle Ages witnessed the rise of <a href="/wiki/Christian_monasticism" title="Christian monasticism">Christian monasticism</a>. The shape of European monasticism was determined by traditions and ideas that originated with the <a href="/wiki/Desert_Fathers" title="Desert Fathers">Desert Fathers</a> of <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>. Monastic ideals spread through <a href="/wiki/Hagiography" title="Hagiography">hagiographical literature</a> such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Anthony_the_Great" title="Anthony the Great">Life of Anthony</a></i>. Most European monasteries were of the type that focuses on community experience of the spiritual life, called <a href="/wiki/Cenobitic_monasticism" title="Cenobitic monasticism">cenobitism</a>, which was pioneered by the Egyptian hermit <a href="/wiki/Pachomius_the_Great" title="Pachomius the Great">Pachomius</a> (d. <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 350</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrence10_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrence10-108">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins75_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins75-109">[104]</a></sup> Bishop <a href="/wiki/Basil_of_Caesarea" title="Basil of Caesarea">Basil of Caesarea</a> (d. 379) wrote a monastic rule for a community of <a href="/wiki/Cappadocia" title="Cappadocia">Cappadocian</a> ascetics which served as a highly esteemed template for similar regulations in the Mediterranean. These mainly covered the spiritual aspects of monasticism. In contrast, the Italian monk <a href="/wiki/Benedict_of_Nursia" title="Benedict of Nursia">Benedict of Nursia</a> (d. 547) adopted a more practical approach, regulating both the administrative and spiritual responsibilities of a community of monks led by an <a href="/wiki/Abbot" title="Abbot">abbot</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Rule_of_Saint_Benedict" title="Rule of Saint Benedict">Benedictine Rule</a> became widely used in western monasteries already before it was decreed the norm for Frankish monastic communities in 817.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrence18_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrence18-110">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins237_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins237-111">[106]</a></sup> In the east, the monastic rules compiled by <a href="/wiki/Theodore_the_Studite" title="Theodore the Studite">Theodore the Studite</a> (d. 826) gained popularity after they were adopted in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lavra" title="Great Lavra">Great Lavra</a>, a newly established imperial monastery on <a href="/wiki/Mount_Athos" title="Mount Athos">Mount Athos</a> in the 960s. The Great Lavra set a precedent for the founding of further Athonite monasteries, turning the mount into the most important centre of Orthodox monasticism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Curta322_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Curta322-112">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>Monks and monasteries had a deep effect on the religious and political life of the Early Middle Ages, in various cases acting as <a href="/wiki/Land_trust" title="Land trust">land trusts</a> for powerful families, centres of propaganda and royal support in newly conquered regions, and bases for missions and proselytisation.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="Wickham does not describe the monasteries as centres of propaganda and royal support. He does not refer to missions and proselytisation. (August 2022)">failed verification</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickham185_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wickham185-113">[108]</a></sup> They were the main and sometimes only outposts of education and literacy in a region. Many of the surviving manuscripts of the Latin <a href="/wiki/Classics#Classical_Rome" title="Classics">classics</a> were copied in monasteries in the Early Middle Ages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hamilton43_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hamilton43-114">[109]</a></sup> Monks were also the authors of new works, including history, theology, and other subjects, written by authors such as <a href="/wiki/Bede" title="Bede">Bede</a> (d. 735), a native of northern England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Colish64_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Colish64-115">[110]</a></sup> The Byzantine missionary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saints_Cyril_and_Methodius" title="Saints Cyril and Methodius">Constantine</a> (d. 869) developed <a href="/wiki/Old_Church_Slavonic" title="Old Church Slavonic">Old Church Slavonic</a> as a new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liturgical_language" title="Liturgical language">liturgical language</a> enriching Slavic vocabulary with Greek religious terms. He also created an alphabet, likely the <a href="/wiki/Glagolitic_script" title="Glagolitic script">Glagolitic script</a>, for it. These innovations established the basis for a flourishing Slavic religious literature. Constantine died as the monk Cyril in a Roman monastery. His work was continued by his brother Methodius (d. 885) and their pupils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Curta183_191_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Curta183_191-116">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carolingian_Europe">Carolingian Europe</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Francia" title="Francia">Francia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Empire" title="Carolingian Empire">Carolingian Empire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Frankish_Empire_481_to_814-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1205" data-file-width="1591" decoding="async" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Frankish_Empire_481_to_814-en.svg/330px-Frankish_Empire_481_to_814-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Frankish_Empire_481_to_814-en.svg/495px-Frankish_Empire_481_to_814-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Frankish_Empire_481_to_814-en.svg/660px-Frankish_Empire_481_to_814-en.svg.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Frankish_Empire_481_to_814-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map showing growth of Frankish power from 481 to 814</div></div></div>
<p>Royal authority was substantially weak in Francia. The Merovingian kings destroyed their own power base by extensive land grants and customarily distributed the kingdom among their sons. In the northeastern Frankish realm <a href="/wiki/Austrasia" title="Austrasia">Austrasia</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arnulfings" title="Arnulfings">Arnulfings</a> were the most prominent beneficiaries of royal favour. As hereditary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mayor_of_the_Palace" title="Mayor of the Palace">Mayors of the Palace</a>, they were the power behind the Austrasian throne from the mid-7th century. The Arnulfings consolidated their authority by keeping their patrimony undivided through generations, and one of them, <a href="/wiki/Pepin_of_Herstal" title="Pepin of Herstal">Pepin of Herstal</a> (d. 714) also assumed power in the central Frankish realm <a href="/wiki/Neustria" title="Neustria">Neustria</a>. His successor <a href="/wiki/Charles_Martel" title="Charles Martel">Charles Martel</a> (d. 741) took advantage of the permanent Muslim threat to confiscate church property and raise new troops by parcelling it out among the recruits. His victory over an expeditionary force from Al-Andalus in the Battle of Tours brought him enormous prestige.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Backman150_154_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Backman150_154-117">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_dynasty" title="Carolingian dynasty">Carolingians</a>, as Charles Martel's descendants are known, succeeded the Merovingians as the new royal dynasty of Francia in 751. This year the last Merovingian king <a href="/wiki/Childeric_III" title="Childeric III">Childeric III</a> (r. 743–751) was deposed, and Charles Martel's son <a href="/wiki/Pepin_the_Short" title="Pepin the Short">Pepin the Short</a> (r. 751–768) was crowned king with the consent of the papacy and the Frankish leaders. Two or three years later <a href="/wiki/Pope_Stephen_II" title="Pope Stephen II">Pope Stephen II</a> (pope 752–757) personally sanctioned the coup by <a href="/wiki/Anointing" title="Anointing">anointing</a> Pepin and his two sons with <a href="/wiki/Chrism" title="Chrism">chrism</a> during his visit to Francia. He came to persuade Pepin to attack the Lombards whose expansion menaced the city of Rome. Pepin defeated the Lombards and enforced their promise to respect the <a href="/wiki/Patrimony_of_Saint_Peter" title="Patrimony of Saint Peter">possessions of the papacy</a>. His subsequent <a href="/wiki/Donation_of_Pepin" title="Donation of Pepin">donation of Central Italian territories</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Holy_See" title="Holy See">Holy See</a> marked the beginnings of the <a href="/wiki/Papal_States" title="Papal States">Papal States</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins276_277_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins276_277-118">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown97_99_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown97_99-119">[114]</a></sup> At the time of his death in 768, Pepin left his kingdom in the hands of his two sons, Charles (r. 768–814) and <a href="/wiki/Carloman_I" title="Carloman I">Carloman</a> (r. 768–771). When Carloman died of natural causes, Charles blocked the succession of Carloman's young son and installed himself as the king of the reunited Francia. Charles, more often known as Charles the Great or <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a>, embarked upon a programme of systematic expansion in 772. In the wars that lasted beyond 800, he rewarded allies with war booty and command over parcels of land. Charlemagne <a href="/wiki/Saxon_Wars" title="Saxon Wars">subjugated the Saxons</a>, conquered the Lombards, and created a <a href="/wiki/Marca_Hispanica" title="Marca Hispanica">new border province</a> in northern Spain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins280_288_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins280_288-120">[115]</a></sup> Between 791 and 803, Frankish troops annihilated the Avars' empire which facilitated the development of small Slavic principalities, mainly ruled by ambitious warlords under Frankish suzerainty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Curta103_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Curta103-121">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[F]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aachener_Dom_Pfalzkapelle_vom_M%C3%BCnsterplatz_2014.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5162" data-file-width="3204" decoding="async" height="274" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Aachener_Dom_Pfalzkapelle_vom_M%C3%BCnsterplatz_2014.jpg/170px-Aachener_Dom_Pfalzkapelle_vom_M%C3%BCnsterplatz_2014.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Aachener_Dom_Pfalzkapelle_vom_M%C3%BCnsterplatz_2014.jpg/255px-Aachener_Dom_Pfalzkapelle_vom_M%C3%BCnsterplatz_2014.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Aachener_Dom_Pfalzkapelle_vom_M%C3%BCnsterplatz_2014.jpg/340px-Aachener_Dom_Pfalzkapelle_vom_M%C3%BCnsterplatz_2014.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aachener_Dom_Pfalzkapelle_vom_M%C3%BCnsterplatz_2014.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Palatine_Chapel,_Aachen" title="Palatine Chapel, Aachen">Charlemagne's palace chapel</a> at <a href="/wiki/Aachen" title="Aachen">Aachen</a>, completed in 805<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stalley73_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stalley73-124">[118]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The coronation of Charlemagne as emperor on Christmas Day 800 is regarded as a turning point in medieval history, marking a return of the Western Roman Empire, since the new emperor ruled over much of the area previously controlled by the Western emperors. It also marks a change in Charlemagne's relationship with the Byzantine Empire, as the assumption of the imperial title by the Carolingians asserted their equivalence to the Byzantine state. In 812, as a result of careful and protracted negotiations, the Byzantines acknowledged Charlemagne's title of "emperor" but without recognizing him as a second "emperor of the Romans", or accepting his successors' claim to use his new title.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Backman109_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Backman109-125">[119]</a></sup> The empire was administered by an itinerant court that travelled with the emperor, as well as approximately 300 imperial officials called <a href="/wiki/Count" title="Count">counts</a>, who administered the <a href="/wiki/County" title="County">counties</a> the empire had been divided into.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies302_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davies302-126">[120]</a></sup> The central administration supervised the counts through imperial emissaries called <i><a href="/wiki/Missus_dominicus" title="Missus dominicus">missi dominici</a></i>, who served as roving inspectors and troubleshooters. The clerics of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_chapel" title="Royal chapel">royal chapel</a> were responsible for recording important royal grants and decisions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins306_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins306-127">[121]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carolingian_Renaissance">Carolingian Renaissance</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Renaissance" title="Carolingian Renaissance">Carolingian Renaissance</a></div>
<p>Charlemagne's court in <a href="/wiki/Aachen" title="Aachen">Aachen</a> was the centre of the cultural revival sometimes referred to as the "<a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Renaissance" title="Carolingian Renaissance">Carolingian Renaissance</a>". Literacy increased, as did development in the arts, architecture and jurisprudence, as well as liturgical and scriptural studies. The English monk <a href="/wiki/Alcuin" title="Alcuin">Alcuin</a> (d. 804) was invited to Aachen and brought the <a href="/wiki/Northumbria%27s_Golden_Age" title="Northumbria's Golden Age">education</a> available in the monasteries of Northumbria. Charlemagne's <a href="/wiki/Chancery_(medieval_office)" title="Chancery (medieval office)">chancery</a>—or writing office—made use of a new <a href="/wiki/Writing_system" title="Writing system">script</a> today known as <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_minuscule" title="Carolingian minuscule">Carolingian minuscule</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[G]</a></sup> allowing a common writing style that advanced communication across much of Europe. Charlemagne sponsored changes in <a href="/wiki/Christian_liturgy" title="Christian liturgy">church liturgy</a>, imposing the Roman form of church service on his domains, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_chant" title="Gregorian chant">Gregorian chant</a> in liturgical music for the churches. An important activity for scholars during this period was the copying, correcting, and dissemination of basic works on religious and secular topics, with the aim of encouraging learning. New works on religious topics and schoolbooks were also produced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Colish66_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Colish66-130">[123]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Linguistics" title="Linguistics">Grammarians</a> of the period modified the Latin language, changing it from the <a href="/wiki/Classical_Latin" title="Classical Latin">Classical Latin</a> of the Roman Empire into a more flexible form to fit the needs of the Church and government. By the reign of Charlemagne, the language had so diverged from the classical Latin that it was later called <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Latin" title="Medieval Latin">Medieval Latin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lang_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lang-131">[124]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breakup_of_the_Carolingian_Empire">Breakup of the Carolingian Empire</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Viking_Age" title="Viking Age">Viking Age</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti class="tsingle" style="width:137px;max-width:137px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carolingian_territorial_divisions,_855.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="594" data-file-width="497" decoding="async" height="161" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Carolingian_territorial_divisions%2C_855.png/270px-Carolingian_territorial_divisions%2C_855.png 2x" width="135"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:137px;max-width:137px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carolingian_territorial_divisions,_870.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="594" data-file-width="497" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Carolingian_territorial_divisions%2C_870.png/135px-Carolingian_territorial_divisions%2C_870.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Carolingian_territorial_divisions%2C_870.png/203px-Carolingian_territorial_divisions%2C_870.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Carolingian_territorial_divisions%2C_870.png/270px-Carolingian_territorial_divisions%2C_870.png 2x" width="135"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Territorial divisions of the <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Empire" title="Carolingian Empire">Carolingian Empire</a> in 843, 855, and 870</div></div></div></div>
<p>Charlemagne planned to continue the Frankish tradition of dividing his kingdom between all his heirs, but was unable to do so as only one son, <a href="/wiki/Louis_the_Pious" title="Louis the Pious">Louis the Pious</a> (r. 814–840), was still alive by 813. Just before Charlemagne died in 814, he crowned Louis as his successor. Louis's reign of 26 years was marked by numerous divisions of the empire among his sons and, after 829, civil wars between various alliances of father and sons over the control of various parts of the empire. Eventually, Louis recognised his eldest son <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Lothair_I" title="Lothair I">Lothair I</a></span> (d. 855) as emperor and gave him the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy_(Holy_Roman_Empire)" title="Kingdom of Italy (Holy Roman Empire)">Kingdom of Italy</a>. Louis divided the rest of the empire between Lothair and <a href="/wiki/Charles_the_Bald" title="Charles the Bald">Charles the Bald</a> (d. 877), his youngest son. Lothair took <a href="/wiki/East_Francia" title="East Francia">East Francia</a>, comprising both banks of the Rhine and eastwards, leaving Charles <a href="/wiki/West_Francia" title="West Francia">West Francia</a> with the empire to the west of the Rhineland and the Alps. <a href="/wiki/Louis_the_German" title="Louis the German">Louis the German</a> (d. 876), the middle child, who had been rebellious to the last, was allowed to keep Bavaria under the <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/suzerainty" title="wikt:suzerainty">suzerainty</a> of his elder brother. The division was disputed. <a href="/wiki/Pepin_II_of_Aquitaine" title="Pepin II of Aquitaine"><span class="nowrap">Pepin II</span> of Aquitaine</a> (d. after 864), the emperor's grandson, rebelled in a contest for <a href="/wiki/Aquitaine" title="Aquitaine">Aquitaine</a>, while Louis the German tried to annex all of East Francia. Louis the Pious died in 840, with the empire still in chaos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bauer427_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bauer427-132">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>A three-year civil war followed his death. By the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Verdun" title="Treaty of Verdun">Treaty of Verdun</a> (843), a kingdom between the <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhone" title="Rhone">Rhone</a> rivers was created for Lothair to go with his lands in Italy, and his imperial title was recognised. Louis the German was in control of Bavaria and the eastern lands in modern-day Germany. Charles the Bald received the western Frankish lands, comprising most of modern-day France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bauer427_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bauer427-132">[125]</a></sup> Charlemagne's grandsons and great-grandsons divided their kingdoms between their descendants, eventually causing all internal cohesion to be lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Backman139_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-Backman139-133">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[H]</a></sup> In 987 the Carolingian dynasty was replaced in the western lands, with the crowning of <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Capet" title="Hugh Capet">Hugh Capet</a> (r. 987–996) as king.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[I]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[J]</a></sup> In the eastern lands the dynasty had died out earlier, in 911, with the death of <a href="/wiki/Louis_the_Child" title="Louis the Child">Louis the Child</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins360_137-2"><a href="#cite_note-Collins360-137">[129]</a></sup> and the selection of the unrelated <a href="/wiki/Conrad_I_of_Germany" title="Conrad I of Germany">Conrad I</a> (r. 911–918) as king.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins397_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins397-140">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>The breakup of the Carolingian Empire was accompanied by invasions, migrations, and raids by external foes. The Atlantic and northern shores were harassed by the <a href="/wiki/Vikings" title="Vikings">Vikings</a>, who also raided the British Isles and settled there as well as in Iceland. In 911, the Viking chieftain <a href="/wiki/Rollo" title="Rollo">Rollo</a> (d. c. 931) received permission from the Frankish King <a href="/wiki/Charles_the_Simple" title="Charles the Simple">Charles the Simple</a> (r. 898–922) to settle in what became <a href="/wiki/Normandy" title="Normandy">Normandy</a>. This settlement eventually expanded and Normans spread to southern Italy, then Sicily and England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Backman141_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Backman141-141">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies336_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davies336-142">[132]</a></sup> The eastern parts of the Frankish kingdoms, especially Germany and Italy, were under continual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hungarian_people" title="Hungarian people">Magyar</a> assault until the invader's defeat at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lechfeld" title="Battle of Lechfeld">Battle of Lechfeld</a> in 955.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Backman144_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Backman144-143">[133]</a></sup> The breakup of the Abbasid dynasty meant that the Islamic world fragmented into smaller political states, some of which began expanding. The Aghlabids <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Sicily" title="Muslim conquest of Sicily">conquered Sicily</a>, the Ummayads of Al-Andalus annexed the <a href="/wiki/Balearic_Islands" title="Balearic Islands">Balearic Islands</a>, and Arab pirates launched regular raids against Italy and southern France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins394_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins394-144">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_kingdoms_and_Byzantine_revival">New kingdoms and Byzantine revival</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire_under_the_Macedonian_dynasty" title="Byzantine Empire under the Macedonian dynasty">Byzantine Empire under the Macedonian dynasty</a>, <a href="/wiki/First_Bulgarian_Empire" title="First Bulgarian Empire">First Bulgarian Empire</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christianisation_of_Bulgaria" title="Christianisation of Bulgaria">Christianisation of Bulgaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Germany" title="Kingdom of Germany">Kingdom of Germany</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christianisation_of_Scandinavia" title="Christianisation of Scandinavia">Christianisation of Scandinavia</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christianisation_of_Kievan_Rus%27" title="Christianisation of Kievan Rus'">Christianisation of Kievan Rus'</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine%E2%80%93Arab_wars_(780%E2%80%931180)" title="Byzantine–Arab wars (780–1180)">Byzantine–Arab wars (780–1180)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Byzantine%E2%80%93Bulgarian_wars" title="Byzantine–Bulgarian wars">Byzantine–Bulgarian wars</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Christ_Magdeburg_Cathedral_Met_41.100.157.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2812" data-file-width="2438" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Christ_Magdeburg_Cathedral_Met_41.100.157.jpg/150px-Christ_Magdeburg_Cathedral_Met_41.100.157.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Christ_Magdeburg_Cathedral_Met_41.100.157.jpg/225px-Christ_Magdeburg_Cathedral_Met_41.100.157.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Christ_Magdeburg_Cathedral_Met_41.100.157.jpg/300px-Christ_Magdeburg_Cathedral_Met_41.100.157.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Christ_Magdeburg_Cathedral_Met_41.100.157.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>10th-century <a href="/wiki/Ottonian_art" title="Ottonian art">Ottonian</a> ivory plaque depicting Christ receiving a church from <span class="nowrap"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Otto_I" title="Otto I">Otto I</a></span></div></div></div>
<p>Efforts by local kings to fight the invaders led to the formation of new political entities. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_England" title="Anglo-Saxon England">Anglo-Saxon England</a>, King <a href="/wiki/Alfred_the_Great" title="Alfred the Great">Alfred the Great</a> (r. 871–899) came to an agreement with the Viking invaders in the late 9th century, resulting in <a href="/wiki/Danelaw" title="Danelaw">Danish settlements</a> in Northumbria, Mercia, and parts of East Anglia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins378_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins378-145">[135]</a></sup> By the middle of the 10th century, Alfred's successors had conquered Northumbria, and restored English control over most of the southern part of Great Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins387_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins387-146">[136]</a></sup> In northern Britain, <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_MacAlpin" title="Kenneth MacAlpin">Kenneth MacAlpin</a> (d. c. 860) united the Picts and the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_people" title="Scottish people">Scots</a> into the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Alba" title="Kingdom of Alba">Kingdom of Alba</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies309_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davies309-147">[137]</a></sup> In the early 10th century, the <a href="/wiki/Ottonian_dynasty" title="Ottonian dynasty">Ottonian dynasty</a> established itself in <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Germany" title="Kingdom of Germany">Germany</a>, and was engaged in driving back the Magyars. Its efforts culminated in the coronation in 962 of <span class="nowrap"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Otto_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Otto I, Holy Roman Emperor">Otto I</a></span> (r. 936–973) as <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Holy Roman Emperor">Holy Roman Emperor</a>. In the mid-10th century Italy was drawn into the Ottonian sphere but the absent German kings could not consolidate royal authority in the Italian realm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins394_411_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins394_411-148">[138]</a></sup> The western Frankish kingdom was more fragmented, and although kings remained nominally in charge, much of the political power devolved to the local lords.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickham439_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wickham439-149">[139]</a></sup> In the Iberian Peninsula, Asturias expanded slowly south in the 8th and 9th centuries, and continued as the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Le%C3%B3n" title="Kingdom of León">Kingdom of León</a> when the royal centre was moved from the northern <a href="/wiki/Oviedo" title="Oviedo">Oviedo</a> to <a href="/wiki/Le%C3%B3n,_Spain" title="León, Spain">León</a> in the 910s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins376_86_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins376_86-150">[140]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Christianization_of_Scandinavia" title="Christianization of Scandinavia">Missionary efforts to Scandinavia</a> during the 9th and 10th centuries helped strengthen the growth of kingdoms such as <a href="/wiki/History_of_Sweden_(800%E2%80%931521)" title="History of Sweden (800–1521)">Sweden</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Denmark" title="History of Denmark">Denmark</a>, and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Norway" title="History of Norway">Norway</a>, which gained power and territory. Some kings converted to Christianity, although not all by 1000. Scandinavians also expanded and colonised throughout Europe. Besides the settlements in Ireland, England, and Normandy, further settlement took place in what became <a href="/wiki/Kievan_Rus%27" title="Kievan Rus'">Russia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Settlement_of_Iceland" title="Settlement of Iceland">Iceland</a>. Swedish traders and raiders ranged down the rivers of the Russian steppe, and even attempted to seize Constantinople in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rus%27%E2%80%93Byzantine_War_(860)" title="Rus'–Byzantine War (860)">860</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rus%27%E2%80%93Byzantine_War_(907)" title="Rus'–Byzantine War (907)">907</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins385_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins385-151">[141]</a></sup> The Eastern European trade routes towards Central Asia and the Near East were controlled by the <a href="/wiki/Khazars" title="Khazars">Khazars</a>. Their multiethnic empire <a href="/wiki/Arab%E2%80%93Khazar_wars" title="Arab–Khazar wars">resisted the Muslim expansion</a>, and the Khazar leaders converted to Judaism by the 830s. The Khazars were nominally ruled by a <a href="/wiki/Sacred_king" title="Sacred king">sacred king</a>, the khagan, but the commander-in-chief of his army, the <a href="/wiki/Bey" title="Bey">beg</a>, was the power behind the throne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Curta131_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-Curta131-152">[142]</a></sup>
</p><p>Byzantium revived its fortunes under Emperor <a href="/wiki/Basil_I" title="Basil I">Basil I</a> (r. 867–886) and his successors <a href="/wiki/Leo_VI_the_Wise" title="Leo VI the Wise">Leo VI</a> (r. 886–912) and <a href="/wiki/Constantine_VII" title="Constantine VII">Constantine VII</a> (r. 913–959), members of the <a href="/wiki/Macedonian_dynasty" title="Macedonian dynasty">Macedonian dynasty</a>. Commerce revived and the emperors oversaw the extension of a uniform administration to all the provinces. The military was reorganised, which allowed the emperors <a href="/wiki/John_I_Tzimiskes" title="John I Tzimiskes">John I</a> (r. 969–976) and <a href="/wiki/Basil_II" title="Basil II">Basil II</a> (r. 976–1025) to expand the frontiers of the empire on all fronts. The imperial court was the centre of a revival of classical learning, a process known as the <a href="/wiki/Macedonian_Renaissance" title="Macedonian Renaissance">Macedonian Renaissance</a>. Writers such as <a href="/wiki/John_Geometres" title="John Geometres">John Geometres</a> (<a href="/wiki/Floruit" title="Floruit">fl.</a> early 10th century) composed new hymns, poems, and other works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies318_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davies318-153">[143]</a></sup> Missionary efforts by both Eastern and Western clergy resulted in the conversion of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moravians_(ethnic_group)" title="Moravians (ethnic group)">Moravians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bulgars" title="Bulgars">Bulgars</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bohemian_(demonym)" title="Bohemian (demonym)">Bohemians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poles" title="Poles">Poles</a>, Magyars, and Slavic<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (January 2022)">failed verification</span></a></i>]</sup> inhabitants of the <a href="/wiki/Kievan_Rus%27" title="Kievan Rus'">Kievan Rus'</a>. These conversions contributed to the founding of political states in the lands of those peoples—the states of <a href="/wiki/Great_Moravia" title="Great Moravia">Moravia</a>, <a href="/wiki/First_Bulgarian_Empire" title="First Bulgarian Empire">Bulgaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bohemia" title="Bohemia">Bohemia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poland_in_the_Early_Middle_Ages" title="Poland in the Early Middle Ages">Poland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hungary_(1000%E2%80%931301)" title="Kingdom of Hungary (1000–1301)">Hungary</a>, and the Kievan Rus'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies321_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davies321-154">[144]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (January 2022)">failed verification</span></a></i>]</sup> Bulgaria, which was founded at the <a href="/wiki/Danube_Delta" title="Danube Delta">Danube Delta</a> around 680, at its height incorporated vast regions along the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lower_Danube" title="Lower Danube">Lower Danube</a>, in the Balkans and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carpathian_Basin" title="Carpathian Basin">Carpathian Basin</a>. By 1018, the last Bulgarian nobles had surrendered to the Byzantine Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Curta72_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-Curta72-155">[145]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art_and_architecture">Art and architecture</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Medieval_art" title="Medieval art">Medieval art</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medieval_architecture" title="Medieval architecture">Medieval architecture</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Migration_Period_art" title="Migration Period art">Migration Period art</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pre-Romanesque_art_and_architecture" title="Pre-Romanesque art and architecture">Pre-Romanesque art and architecture</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_art" title="Carolingian art">Carolingian art</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KellsFol032vChristEnthroned.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1934" data-file-width="1484" decoding="async" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/KellsFol032vChristEnthroned.jpg/170px-KellsFol032vChristEnthroned.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/KellsFol032vChristEnthroned.jpg/255px-KellsFol032vChristEnthroned.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/KellsFol032vChristEnthroned.jpg/340px-KellsFol032vChristEnthroned.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KellsFol032vChristEnthroned.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A page from the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Kells" title="Book of Kells">Book of Kells</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Illuminated_manuscript" title="Illuminated manuscript">illuminated manuscript</a> created in the British Isles in the late 8th or early 9th century<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nees145_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nees145-156">[146]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Milan" title="Edict of Milan">Edict of Milan</a> legalized Christianity and Judaism in the Roman Empire, new public places of worship emerged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stalley21_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stalley21-157">[147]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Basilica" title="Basilica">Basilicas</a>, large halls that originally served administrative functions, were adapted for Christian worship under Constantine the Great.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[148]</a></sup> During his successors' reign, new basilicas were built in the major cities of the Roman world, and even in the post-Roman tribal kingdoms until the mid-6th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[K]</a></sup> As the spacious basilicas became of little use with the decline of urban centres, they gave way to smaller churches, mainly divided into little chambers. By the beginning of the 8th century, the Carolingian Empire revived the basilica form of architecture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stalley219_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stalley219-161">[150]</a></sup> One feature of the basilica is the use of a <a href="/wiki/Transept" title="Transept">transept</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stalley43_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stalley43-162">[151]</a></sup> or the "arms" of a cross-shaped building that are perpendicular to the long <a href="/wiki/Nave" title="Nave">nave</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cosman247_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cosman247-163">[152]</a></sup> Other new features of religious architecture include the <a href="/wiki/Crossing_(architecture)" title="Crossing (architecture)">crossing tower</a> and a monumental <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_cathedrals_and_great_churches#Façade" title="Architecture of cathedrals and great churches">entrance to the church</a>, usually at the west end of the building.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stalley49_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stalley49-164">[153]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Carolingian_art" title="Carolingian art">Carolingian art</a> was produced for a small group of figures around the court, and the monasteries and churches they supported. It was dominated by efforts to regain the dignity and classicism of imperial Roman and <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_art" title="Byzantine art">Byzantine art</a>, but was also influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Insular_art" title="Insular art">Insular art</a> of the British Isles. Insular art integrated the energy of <a href="/wiki/Celtic_art" title="Celtic art">Irish Celtic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_art" title="Anglo-Saxon art">Anglo-Saxon Germanic</a> styles of ornament with Mediterranean forms such as the book, and established many characteristics of art for the rest of the medieval period. Surviving religious works from the Early Middle Ages are mostly <a href="/wiki/Illuminated_manuscript" title="Illuminated manuscript">illuminated manuscripts</a> and carved <a href="/wiki/Ivory_carving#Antiquity_and_the_Early_Medieval_period" title="Ivory carving">ivories</a>, originally made for metalwork that has since been melted down.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kitzinger36_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kitzinger36-165">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Henderson18_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-Henderson18-166">[155]</a></sup> Objects in precious metals were the most prestigious form of art, but almost all are lost except for a few crosses such as the <a href="/wiki/Cross_of_Lothair" title="Cross of Lothair">Cross of Lothair</a>, several <a href="/wiki/Reliquary" title="Reliquary">reliquaries</a>, and finds such as the Anglo-Saxon burial at <a href="/wiki/Sutton_Hoo" title="Sutton Hoo">Sutton Hoo</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Hoard" title="Hoard">hoards</a> of <a href="/wiki/Treasure_of_Gourdon" title="Treasure of Gourdon">Gourdon</a> from Merovingian France, <a href="/wiki/Treasure_of_Guarrazar" title="Treasure of Guarrazar">Guarrazar</a> from Visigothic Spain and <a href="/wiki/Treasure_of_Nagyszentmikl%C3%B3s" title="Treasure of Nagyszentmiklós">Nagyszentmiklós</a> near Byzantine territory. There are survivals from the large <a href="/wiki/Brooch" title="Brooch">brooches</a> in <a href="/wiki/Fibula_(brooch)" title="Fibula (brooch)">fibula</a> or <a href="/wiki/Celtic_brooch" title="Celtic brooch">penannular</a> form that were a key piece of personal adornment for elites, including the Irish <a href="/wiki/Tara_Brooch" title="Tara Brooch">Tara Brooch</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Henderson36_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-Henderson36-167">[156]</a></sup> Highly decorated books were mostly <a href="/wiki/Gospel_Book" title="Gospel Book">Gospel Books</a> and these have survived in <a href="/wiki/List_of_illuminated_manuscripts" title="List of illuminated manuscripts">larger numbers</a>, including the Insular <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Kells" title="Book of Kells">Book of Kells</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Lindisfarne_Gospels" title="Lindisfarne Gospels">Book of Lindisfarne</a>, and the imperial <a href="/wiki/Codex_Aureus_of_St._Emmeram" title="Codex Aureus of St. Emmeram">Codex Aureus of St. Emmeram</a>, which is one of the few to retain its "<a href="/wiki/Treasure_binding" title="Treasure binding">treasure binding</a>" of gold encrusted with jewels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Benton41_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-Benton41-168">[157]</a></sup> Charlemagne's court seems to have been responsible for the acceptance of figurative <a href="/wiki/Monumental_sculpture" title="Monumental sculpture">monumental sculpture</a> in <a href="/wiki/Christian_art" title="Christian art">Christian art</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lasko16_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lasko16-169">[158]</a></sup> and by the end of the period near life-sized figures such as the <a href="/wiki/Gero_Cross" title="Gero Cross">Gero Cross</a> were common in important churches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Henderson233_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-Henderson233-170">[159]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_and_technological_developments">Military and technological developments</span></h3>
<p>During the later Roman Empire, the principal military developments were attempts to create an effective cavalry force as well as the continued development of highly specialised types of troops. The creation of heavily armoured <a href="/wiki/Cataphract" title="Cataphract">cataphract</a>-type soldiers as cavalry was an important feature of the 5th-century Roman military. The various invading tribes had differing emphases on types of soldiers—ranging from the primarily infantry Anglo-Saxon invaders of Britain to the Vandals and Visigoths who had a high proportion of cavalry in their armies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NicolleWestern28_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-NicolleWestern28-171">[160]</a></sup> During the early invasion period, the <a href="/wiki/Stirrup" title="Stirrup">stirrup</a> had not been introduced into warfare, which limited the usefulness of cavalry as <a href="/wiki/Shock_troops" title="Shock troops">shock troops</a> because it was not possible to put the full force of the horse and rider behind blows struck by the rider.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NicolleWestern30_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-NicolleWestern30-172">[161]</a></sup> The greatest change in military affairs during the invasion period was the adoption of the Hunnic <a href="/wiki/Composite_bow" title="Composite bow">composite bow</a> in place of the earlier, and weaker, <a href="/wiki/Scythia" title="Scythia">Scythian</a> composite bow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NicolleWestern31_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-NicolleWestern31-173">[162]</a></sup> Another development was the increasing use of <a href="/wiki/Longsword" title="Longsword">longswords</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NicolleWestern34_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-NicolleWestern34-174">[163]</a></sup> and the progressive replacement of <a href="/wiki/Scale_armour" title="Scale armour">scale armour</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mail_(armour)" title="Mail (armour)">mail armour</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lamellar_armour" title="Lamellar armour">lamellar armour</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NicolleWestern39_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-NicolleWestern39-175">[164]</a></sup>
</p><p>The importance of infantry and light cavalry began to decline during the early Carolingian period, with a growing dominance of elite heavy cavalry. The use of <a href="/wiki/Conscription" title="Conscription">militia-type levies</a> of the free population declined over the Carolingian period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NicolleWestern58_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-NicolleWestern58-176">[165]</a></sup> Although much of the Carolingian armies were mounted, a large proportion during the early period appear to have been <a href="/wiki/Mounted_infantry" title="Mounted infantry">mounted infantry</a>, rather than true cavalry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NicolleWestern76_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-NicolleWestern76-177">[166]</a></sup> One exception was Anglo-Saxon England, where the armies were still composed of regional levies, known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Fyrd" title="Fyrd">fyrd</a></i>, which were led by the local elites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NicolleWestern67_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-NicolleWestern67-178">[167]</a></sup> In military technology, one of the main changes was the return of the <a href="/wiki/Crossbow" title="Crossbow">crossbow</a>, which had been known in Roman times and reappeared as a military weapon during the last part of the Early Middle Ages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NicolleWestern80_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-NicolleWestern80-179">[168]</a></sup> Another change was the introduction of the stirrup, which increased the effectiveness of cavalry as shock troops. A technological advance that had implications beyond the military was the <a href="/wiki/Horseshoe" title="Horseshoe">horseshoe</a>, which allowed horses to be used in rocky terrain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NicolleWestern88_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-NicolleWestern88-180">[169]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="High_Middle_Ages">High Middle Ages</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/High_Middle_Ages" title="High Middle Ages">High Middle Ages</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_economic_life">Society and economic life</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Agriculture in the Middle Ages">Agriculture in the Middle Ages</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cleric-Knight-Workman.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="672" data-file-width="656" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Cleric-Knight-Workman.jpg/220px-Cleric-Knight-Workman.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Cleric-Knight-Workman.jpg/330px-Cleric-Knight-Workman.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Cleric-Knight-Workman.jpg/440px-Cleric-Knight-Workman.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cleric-Knight-Workman.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Medieval French <a href="/wiki/Illuminated_manuscript" title="Illuminated manuscript">manuscript illustration</a> of the three <a href="/wiki/Social_class" title="Social class">classes</a> of medieval society: those who prayed (the <a href="/wiki/Clergy" title="Clergy">clergy</a>) those who fought (the <a href="/wiki/Knight" title="Knight">knights</a>), and those who worked (the <a href="/wiki/Peasant" title="Peasant">peasantry</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whitton134_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-Whitton134-181">[170]</a></sup> The relationship between these classes was governed by <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manorialism" title="Manorialism">manorialism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gainty352_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gainty352-182">[171]</a></sup> (<i>Li Livres dou Sante</i>, 13th century)</div></div></div>
<p>The High Middle Ages was a period of tremendous <a href="/wiki/Medieval_demography" title="Medieval demography">expansion of population</a>. The estimated population of Europe grew from 35 to 80 million between 1000 and 1347, although the exact causes remain unclear: improved agricultural techniques, the decline of slaveholding, a <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Warm_Period" title="Medieval Warm Period">more clement climate</a> and the lack of invasion have all been suggested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jordan5_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jordan5-183">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Backman156_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-Backman156-184">[173]</a></sup> As much as 90 per cent of the European population remained rural peasants. Many were no longer settled in isolated farms but had gathered into small communities, usually known as  <a href="/wiki/Manorialism" title="Manorialism">manors</a> or villages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Backman156_184-1"><a href="#cite_note-Backman156-184">[173]</a></sup> These peasants were often subject to noble overlords and owed them rents and other services, in a system known as <a href="/wiki/Manorialism" title="Manorialism">manorialism</a>. There remained a few free peasants throughout this period and beyond,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Backman164_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-Backman164-185">[174]</a></sup> with more of them in the regions of Southern Europe than in the north. The practice of <a href="/wiki/Assarting" title="Assarting">assarting</a>, or bringing new lands into production by offering incentives to the peasants who settled them, also contributed to the expansion of population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Epstein52_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-Epstein52-186">[175]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Open-field_system" title="Open-field system">open-field system</a> of agriculture was commonly practiced in most of Europe, especially in "northwestern and central Europe".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pounds166_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pounds166-187">[176]</a></sup> Such agricultural communities had three basic characteristics: individual peasant holdings in the form of strips of land were scattered among the different fields belonging to the manor; crops were rotated from year to year to preserve soil fertility; and common land was used for grazing livestock and other purposes. Some regions used a three-field system of crop rotation, others retained the older two-field system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Agri_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-Agri-188">[177]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other sections of society included the nobility, clergy, and townsmen. Nobles, both the titled <a href="/wiki/Nobility" title="Nobility">nobility</a> and simple <a href="/wiki/Knight" title="Knight">knights</a>, exploited the manors and the peasants, although they did not own lands outright but were granted rights to the income from a manor or other lands by an overlord through the system of <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudalism</a>. During the 11th and 12th centuries, these lands, or <a href="/wiki/Fief" title="Fief">fiefs</a>, came to be considered hereditary, and in most areas they were no longer divisible between all the heirs as had been the case in the early medieval period. Instead, most fiefs and lands went to the eldest son.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barber37_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barber37-189">[178]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[L]</a></sup> The dominance of the nobility was built upon its control of the land, its military service as <a href="/wiki/Heavy_cavalry" title="Heavy cavalry">heavy cavalry</a>, control of <a href="/wiki/Castle" title="Castle">castles</a>, and various immunities from taxes or other impositions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[M]</a></sup> Castles, initially in wood but later in stone, began to be constructed in the 9th and 10th centuries in response to the disorder of the time, and provided protection from invaders as well as allowing lords defence from rivals. Control of castles allowed the nobles to defy kings or other overlords.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies311_192-1"><a href="#cite_note-Davies311-192">[180]</a></sup> Nobles were stratified; kings and the highest-ranking nobility controlled large numbers of commoners and large tracts of land, as well as other nobles. Beneath them, lesser aristocrats had authority over smaller areas of land and fewer people, often only commoners. The lowest-ranking nobles did not own land, and had to serve wealthier aristocrats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daily3_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-Daily3-194">[181]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[N]</a></sup>
</p><p>The clergy was divided into two types: the <a href="/wiki/Secular_clergy" title="Secular clergy">secular clergy</a>, who lived out in the world, and the <a href="/wiki/Regular_clergy" title="Regular clergy">regular clergy</a>, who lived isolated under a religious rule and usually consisted of monks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hamilton33_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hamilton33-197">[183]</a></sup> Throughout the period monks remained a very small proportion of the population, usually less than one percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daily143_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-Daily143-198">[184]</a></sup> Most of the regular clergy were drawn from the nobility, the same social class that served as the recruiting ground for the upper levels of the secular clergy. The local <a href="/wiki/Parish" title="Parish">parish</a> priests were often drawn from the peasant class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barber33_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barber33-199">[185]</a></sup> Townsmen were in a somewhat unusual position, as they did not fit into the traditional three-fold division of society into nobles, clergy, and peasants. During the 12th and 13th centuries, the ranks of the townsmen expanded greatly as existing towns grew and new population centres were founded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barber48_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barber48-200">[186]</a></sup> But throughout the Middle Ages the population of the towns probably never exceeded 10 percent of the total population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daily171_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-Daily171-201">[187]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Petrus_alphonsi_dialogues.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1910" data-file-width="1510" decoding="async" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Petrus_alphonsi_dialogues.jpg/170px-Petrus_alphonsi_dialogues.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Petrus_alphonsi_dialogues.jpg/255px-Petrus_alphonsi_dialogues.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Petrus_alphonsi_dialogues.jpg/340px-Petrus_alphonsi_dialogues.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Petrus_alphonsi_dialogues.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>13th-century illustration of a Jew (in pointed <a href="/wiki/Jewish_hat" title="Jewish hat">Jewish hat</a>) and the Christian <a href="/wiki/Petrus_Alphonsi" title="Petrus Alphonsi">Petrus Alphonsi</a> debating</div></div></div>
<p>Jews also <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jews_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Jews in the Middle Ages">spread across Europe</a> during the period. Communities were established in <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Germany" title="History of the Jews in Germany">Germany</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_England_(1066%E2%80%931290)" title="History of the Jews in England (1066–1290)">England</a> in the 11th and 12th centuries, but <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Spain#Toleration_and_Jewish_immigration_(1085–1212)" title="History of the Jews in Spain">Spanish Jews</a>, long settled in Spain under the Muslims, came under Christian rule and increasing pressure to convert to Christianity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jews191_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jews191-89">[84]</a></sup> Most Jews were confined to the cities, as they were not allowed to own land or be peasants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Epstein54_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-Epstein54-202">[188]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[O]</a></sup> Besides the Jews, there were other non-Christians on the edges of Europe—pagan Slavs in Eastern Europe and Muslims in Southern Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daily13_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-Daily13-204">[189]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Women_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Women in the Middle Ages">Women in the Middle Ages</a> were officially required to be subordinate to some male, whether their father, husband, or other kinsman. Widows, who were often allowed much control over their own lives, were still restricted legally. Women's work generally consisted of household or other domestically inclined tasks. Peasant women were usually responsible for taking care of the household, child-care, as well as gardening and animal husbandry near the house. They could supplement the household income by spinning or brewing at home. At harvest-time, they were also expected to help with field-work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daily14_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-Daily14-205">[190]</a></sup> Townswomen, like peasant women, were responsible for the household, and could also engage in trade. What trades were open to women varied by country and period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daily177_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-Daily177-206">[191]</a></sup> Noblewomen were responsible for running a household, and could occasionally be expected to handle estates in the absence of male relatives, but they were usually restricted from participation in military or government affairs. The only role open to women in the Church was that of <a href="/wiki/Nun" title="Nun">nuns</a>, as they were unable to become priests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daily14_205-1"><a href="#cite_note-Daily14-205">[190]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Central_Italy" title="Central Italy">central</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northern_Italy" title="Northern Italy">northern Italy</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Flanders" title="Flanders">Flanders</a>, the rise of towns that were to a degree self-governing stimulated economic growth and created an environment for new types of trade associations. Commercial cities on the shores of the Baltic entered into agreements known as the <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic League</a>, and the Italian <a href="/wiki/Maritime_republics" title="Maritime republics">Maritime republics</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Genoa" title="Genoa">Genoa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pisa" title="Pisa">Pisa</a> expanded their trade throughout the Mediterranean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[P]</a></sup> Great <a href="/wiki/Fair" title="Fair">trading fairs</a> were established and flourished in <a href="/wiki/Champagne_fairs" title="Champagne fairs">northern France</a> during the period, allowing Italian and German merchants to trade with each other as well as local merchants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Epstein82_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-Epstein82-209">[193]</a></sup> In the late 13th century new land and sea routes to the Far East were pioneered, famously described in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Travels_of_Marco_Polo" title="The Travels of Marco Polo">The Travels of Marco Polo</a></i> written by one of the traders, <a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">Marco Polo</a> (d. 1324).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barber60_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barber60-210">[194]</a></sup> Besides new trading opportunities, agricultural and technological improvements enabled an increase in crop yields, which in turn allowed the trade networks to expand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Backman160_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-Backman160-211">[195]</a></sup> Rising trade brought new methods of dealing with money, and gold coinage was again minted in Europe, first in Italy and later in France and other countries. New forms of commercial contracts emerged, allowing risk to be shared among merchants. Accounting methods improved, partly through the use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Double-entry_bookkeeping_system" title="Double-entry bookkeeping system">double-entry bookkeeping</a>; <a href="/wiki/Letter_of_credit" title="Letter of credit">letters of credit</a> also appeared, allowing easy transmission of money.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barber74_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barber74-212">[196]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rise_of_state_power">Rise of state power</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/England_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="England in the Middle Ages">England in the Middle Ages</a>, <a href="/wiki/France_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="France in the Middle Ages">France in the Middle Ages</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germany_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Germany in the Middle Ages">Germany in the Middle Ages</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Italy in the Middle Ages">Italy in the Middle Ages</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Spain in the Middle Ages">Spain in the Middle Ages</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Poland_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Poland in the Middle Ages">Poland in the Middle Ages</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Europe_mediterranean_1190.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1810" data-file-width="2293" decoding="async" height="229" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Europe_mediterranean_1190.jpg/290px-Europe_mediterranean_1190.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Europe_mediterranean_1190.jpg/435px-Europe_mediterranean_1190.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Europe_mediterranean_1190.jpg/580px-Europe_mediterranean_1190.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Europe_mediterranean_1190.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Europe and the Mediterranean Sea in 1190</div></div></div>
<p>The High Middle Ages was the formative period in the history of the modern Western state. Kings in France, England, and Spain consolidated their power, and set up lasting governing institutions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Backman283_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-Backman283-213">[197]</a></sup> New kingdoms such as <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hungary" title="Kingdom of Hungary">Hungary</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Poland_(1025%E2%80%931385)" title="Kingdom of Poland (1025–1385)">Poland</a>, after their conversion to Christianity, became Central European powers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barber365_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barber365-214">[198]</a></sup> The Magyars <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_conquest_of_the_Carpathian_Basin" title="Hungarian conquest of the Carpathian Basin">settled Hungary</a> around 900 after a series of invasions in the 9th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies296_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davies296-215">[199]</a></sup> which resulted the disintegration of Moravia and the cessation of the <a href="/wiki/March_of_Pannonia" title="March of Pannonia">rule</a> of East Francia beyond the <a href="/wiki/Enns_(river)" title="Enns (river)">Enns river</a>. The papacy, long attached to an ideology of independence from secular kings, first asserted its claim to temporal authority over the entire Christian world; the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temporal_power_(papal)" title="Temporal power (papal)">Papal Monarchy</a> reached its apogee in the early 13th century under the pontificate of <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Pope_Innocent_III" title="Pope Innocent III">Innocent III</a></span> (pope 1198–1216).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Backman262_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-Backman262-216">[200]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Northern_Crusades" title="Northern Crusades">Northern Crusades</a> and the advance of Christian kingdoms and military orders into previously <a href="/wiki/Paganism" title="Paganism">pagan</a> regions in the Baltic and <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finnic</a> north-east brought the forced assimilation of numerous native peoples into European culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barber371_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barber371-217">[201]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the early High Middle Ages, Germany was ruled by the <a href="/wiki/Ottonian_dynasty" title="Ottonian dynasty">Ottonian dynasty</a>, which struggled to control the powerful dukes ruling over <a href="/wiki/Stem_duchy" title="Stem duchy">territorial duchies</a> tracing back to the Migration period. In 1024, they were replaced by the <a href="/wiki/Salian_dynasty" title="Salian dynasty">Salian dynasty</a>, who famously clashed with the papacy under Emperor <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Henry_IV,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Henry IV, Holy Roman Emperor">Henry IV</a></span> (r. 1084–1105) over Church appointments as part of the <a href="/wiki/Investiture_Controversy" title="Investiture Controversy">Investiture Controversy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Backman181_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-Backman181-218">[202]</a></sup> His successors continued to struggle against the papacy as well as the German nobility. A period of instability followed the death of Emperor <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Henry_V,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Henry V, Holy Roman Emperor">Henry V</a></span> (r. 1111–25), who died without heirs, until <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frederick_I_Barbarossa" title="Frederick I Barbarossa"><span class="nowrap">Frederick I</span> Barbarossa</a> (r. 1155–90) took the imperial throne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jordan143_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jordan143-219">[203]</a></sup> Although he ruled effectively, the basic problems remained, and his successors continued to struggle into the 13th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jordan250_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jordan250-220">[204]</a></sup> Barbarossa's grandson <a href="/wiki/Frederick_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Frederick II, Holy Roman Emperor">Frederick II</a> (r. 1220–50), who was also heir to the throne of Sicily through his mother, clashed repeatedly with the papacy. His court was famous for its scholars and he was often accused of <a href="/wiki/Heresy_in_Christianity" title="Heresy in Christianity">heresy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Denley235_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-Denley235-221">[205]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bayeux_Tapestry_scene44_William_Odo_Robert.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2596" data-file-width="2346" decoding="async" height="243" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Bayeux_Tapestry_scene44_William_Odo_Robert.jpg/220px-Bayeux_Tapestry_scene44_William_Odo_Robert.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Bayeux_Tapestry_scene44_William_Odo_Robert.jpg/330px-Bayeux_Tapestry_scene44_William_Odo_Robert.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Bayeux_Tapestry_scene44_William_Odo_Robert.jpg/440px-Bayeux_Tapestry_scene44_William_Odo_Robert.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bayeux_Tapestry_scene44_William_Odo_Robert.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Bayeux_Tapestry" title="Bayeux Tapestry">Bayeux Tapestry</a> (detail) showing <a href="/wiki/William_the_Conqueror" title="William the Conqueror">William the Conqueror</a> (centre), his half-brothers <a href="/wiki/Robert,_Count_of_Mortain" title="Robert, Count of Mortain">Robert, Count of Mortain</a> (right) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Odo,_Earl_of_Kent" title="Odo, Earl of Kent">Odo</a>, Bishop of <a href="/wiki/Bayeux" title="Bayeux">Bayeux</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Normandy" title="Duchy of Normandy">Duchy of Normandy</a> (left)</div></div></div>
<p>Under the <a href="/wiki/Capetian_dynasty" title="Capetian dynasty">Capetian dynasty</a> the French monarchy slowly began to expand its authority over the nobility, growing out of the <a href="/wiki/%C3%8Ele-de-France" title="Île-de-France">Île-de-France</a> to exert control over more of the country in the 11th and 12th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Backman187_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-Backman187-222">[206]</a></sup> They faced a powerful rival in the <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Normandy" title="Duke of Normandy">Dukes of Normandy</a>, who in 1066 under <a href="/wiki/William_the_Conqueror" title="William the Conqueror">William the Conqueror</a> (duke 1035–1087), conquered England (r. 1066–87) and created a cross-channel empire that lasted, in various forms, throughout the rest of the Middle Ages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jordan59_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jordan59-223">[207]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Backman189_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-Backman189-224">[208]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Italo-Normans" title="Italo-Normans">Norman</a> warbands <a href="/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_southern_Italy" title="Norman conquest of southern Italy">seized southern Italy</a> and Sicily from the local Lombard, Byzantine and Muslim rulers. Their hold of the territory <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Melfi" title="Treaty of Melfi">was recognised</a> by the papacy in 1059, and <a href="/wiki/Roger_II_of_Sicily" title="Roger II of Sicily">Roger II</a> (r. 1105–54) united these lands into the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Sicily" title="Kingdom of Sicily">Kingdom of Sicily</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barber206_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barber206-225">[209]</a></sup> Under the <a href="/wiki/Angevin_kings_of_England" title="Angevin kings of England">Angevin dynasty</a> of <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_England" title="Henry II of England">Henry II</a></span> (r. 1154–89) and his son <a href="/wiki/Richard_I_of_England" title="Richard I of England">Richard I</a> (r. 1189–99), the kings of England ruled over England and large areas of France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Backman263_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-Backman263-226">[210]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[Q]</a></sup> Richard's younger brother <a href="/wiki/John,_King_of_England" title="John, King of England">John</a> (r. 1199–1216) lost Normandy and the rest of the northern French possessions in 1204 to the French King <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_France" title="Philip II of France">Philip II Augustus</a> (r. 1180–1223). This led to dissension among the English nobility, while John's financial exactions to pay for his unsuccessful attempts to regain Normandy led in 1215 to <i><a href="/wiki/Magna_Carta" title="Magna Carta">Magna Carta</a></i>, a charter that confirmed the rights and privileges of free men in England. Under <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Henry_III_of_England" title="Henry III of England">Henry III</a></span> (r. 1216–72), John's son, further concessions were made to the nobility, and royal power was diminished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Backman286_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-Backman286-229">[212]</a></sup> The French monarchy continued to make gains against the nobility during the late 12th and 13th centuries, bringing more territories within the kingdom under the king's personal rule and centralising the royal administration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Backman289_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-Backman289-230">[213]</a></sup> Under <a href="/wiki/Louis_IX_of_France" title="Louis IX of France">Louis IX</a> (r. 1226–70), royal prestige rose to new heights as Louis served as a mediator for most of Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies355_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davies355-231">[214]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[R]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Iberia, the Christian states, which had been confined to the north-western part of the peninsula, began to push back against the Islamic states in the south, a period known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Reconquista" title="Reconquista">Reconquista</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies345_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davies345-234">[216]</a></sup> By about 1150, the Christian north had coalesced into the five major kingdoms of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Le%C3%B3n" title="Kingdom of León">León</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Castile" title="Kingdom of Castile">Castile</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Aragon" title="Kingdom of Aragon">Aragon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Navarre" title="Kingdom of Navarre">Navarre</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Portugal" title="Kingdom of Portugal">Portugal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barber341_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barber341-235">[217]</a></sup> Southern Iberia remained under control of Islamic states, initially under the <a href="/wiki/Caliphate_of_C%C3%B3rdoba" title="Caliphate of Córdoba">Caliphate of Córdoba</a>, which broke up in 1031 into a shifting number of petty states known as <i><a href="/wiki/Taifa" title="Taifa">taifas</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies345_234-1"><a href="#cite_note-Davies345-234">[216]</a></sup> Although the <a href="/wiki/Almoravid_dynasty" title="Almoravid dynasty">Almoravids</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Almohad_Caliphate" title="Almohad Caliphate">Almohads</a>, two dynasties from the Maghreb, established centralised rule over Southern Iberia in the 1110s and 1170s respectively, their empires quickly disintegrated. Christian forces advanced again in the early 13th century, culminating in the capture of <a href="/wiki/Seville" title="Seville">Seville</a> in 1248.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barber350_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barber350-236">[218]</a></sup>
</p><p>With the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a> in the Eurasian steppes under <a href="/wiki/Genghis_Khan" title="Genghis Khan">Genghis Khan</a> (r. 1206–27), a new expansionist power reached Europe's eastern borderlands. Convinced of their heavenly sanctioned mission to conquer the world, the Mongols used extreme violence to overcome all resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barber458_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barber458-237">[219]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasion_of_Europe" title="Mongol invasion of Europe">Between 1236 and 1242</a>, they conquered <a href="/wiki/Volga_Bulgaria" title="Volga Bulgaria">Volga Bulgaria</a>, shattered the Kievan Rus' principalities, and laid waste to large regions in Poland, Hungary, Croatia, Serbia and Bulgaria. Their commander-in-chief <a href="/wiki/Batu_Khan" title="Batu Khan">Batu Khan</a> (r. 1241–56)—a grandson of Genghis Khan—set up his capital at <a href="/wiki/Sarai_(city)" title="Sarai (city)">Sarai</a> on the Volga, establishing the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Horde" title="Golden Horde">Golden Horde</a>, a Mongol state nominally under the distant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Khan" title="Great Khan">Great Khan</a>'s authority. The Mongols extracted heavy tribute from the Rus' principalities, and the Rus' princes had to ingratiate themselves with the Mongol khans for economic and political concessions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[S]</a></sup> The Mongol conquest was followed by a peaceful period in Eastern Europe. This <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Pax_Mongolica" title="Pax Mongolica">Pax Mongolica</a></i></span> facilitated the development of direct trade contacts between Europe and China through newly established Genoese colonies in the Black Sea region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Curta703_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-Curta703-240">[221]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crusades">Crusades</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Crusades</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reconquista" title="Reconquista">Reconquista</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Northern_Crusades" title="Northern Crusades">Northern Crusades</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire_under_the_Macedonian_dynasty" title="Byzantine Empire under the Macedonian dynasty">Byzantine Empire under the Macedonian dynasty</a> and <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire_under_the_Komnenos_dynasty" title="Byzantine Empire under the Komnenos dynasty">Byzantine Empire under the Komnenos dynasty</a></div>
